The time and manner of payment of duty will have to be in accordance with the provisions of Rules 9 and 9A(4).	None
There is no other duty charged under the Central Excise Act and there is no other way a duty can be paid under the Central Excise Act.	None
It is the person who has paid the duty of Central excise under the charge imposed by the Act and within the time and in the manner laid down by the Act, who can claim the refund of duty under Section	None
11B.	Decision
Any person claiming refund of any duty of excise must be the person who has paid the aforesaid duty in the aforesaid manner.	None
A consumer or buyer cannot say that he has paid any duty of excise.	None
The duty is only on the manufacturer and not on the consumer.	None
Under subsection (2), the Excise Officer has to be satisfied that the whole or any part of the duty of excise should be refunded to the person who has paid the duty.	None
This is the law in respect of payment of duty and obtaining refund of duty paid in excess.	None
The buyer or the consumer does not pay any duty and, therefore, he is precluded from making any application for refund under Section	None
11B.	Decision
A person who has not paid any duty in law cannot claim a refund on the ground that he has borne the burden of duty.	None
The Excise Officer is a creature of the statute.	None
His powers and functions are circumscribed by the statute.	None
He can realise tax strictly in accordance with the statute.	None
He cannot realise tax beyond the charge imposed by Section 3 out of any extrastatutory considerations.	None
If more tax than permissible under the charge imposed by Section 3 has been collected, it must be returned to the taxpayer.	None
There is nothing in the Act which enables the Excise Officer to embark upon an enquiry to find out whether after payment of the duty, the manufacturer has sold his goods and if so, has included this amount in his price.	None
It is not a ground on which the Excise Officer can refuse to refund the excess amount of duty paid by the manufacturer in the mode and manner laid down by the Act.	None
A taxation statute has to be construed strictly.	None
The Excise Officer cannot insert a proviso to the section and say that even if the levy is illegal and the manufacturer is otherwise entitled to refund of duty under Section 11B, he will not be given this refund if he has included the duty element in the price of the goods manufactured by him.	None
The Excise Officer has no discretionary power to refuse to pay refund even when he was satisfied that excess payment of duty contrary to law has been collected or paid.	None
Though subsection (2) of Section 11B or earlier Rule 11A used the language that the Central Excise Officer may make an order of refund, the word may, in this context, has to be construed as must.	None
The section does not give the Central Excise Officer any discretion once he was satisfied that excess payment had been made.	None
He cannot withhold payment on some extraneous reasons.	None
This point was dealt with at length in the Australian case of Commr. of State Revenue v. Royal Insurance by Dawson, J. There, Section 111(1) of the Stamps Act, provided Where the Comptroller finds in any case that duty has been overpaid, whether before or after the commencement of the Stamps Act, 1978 he may refund to the company, person or firm of persons which or who paid the duty the amount of duty found to be overpaid.	None
This section was later on amended to provide that the Comptroller must refund the amount of the overpaid duty upon an application made within three years of overpayment.	None
There was no dispute that a huge amount of stamp duty had been overpaid by Royal in respect of premiums for workers compensation insurance.	None
The overpayments had been passed on.	None
The Comptroller made a decision not to refund the overpaid duty.	None
Royal initiated an action for the recovery of the amount.	None
It was unsuccessful before the trial Judge who reached the conclusion that the use of the word may in Section 111(1) gave the Comptroller a discretion whether or not to refund the overpaid tax.	None
The Full Court on appeal came to a contrary conclusion.	None
It held that after being satisfied that overpayment had been made, it was not open to the Comptroller to refuse to refund the duty.	None
One of the points argued was that the Act was amended later to use the word shall in place of may.	None
Dawson, J. observed that this was of no consequence.	None
On behalf of the Comptroller it was argued that a number of considerations might justify her withholding of refund of overpaid stamp duty and submitted that the possibility of these situations arising explains why the legislature had used the word may.	None
Chief among these considerations was the impossibility of ensuring that where the duty had been passed on to some other person, any refund should be similarly passed on.	None
It was argued that unlikelihood of Royals passing on of any refund would result in a windfall to it because the burden of the duty had in fact been borne by its customers.	None
Dawson, J. repelled this contention by saying But that it is a situation for which the legislature might have provided had it wished to do so and its failure to do so does not indicate an intention to give to the Comptroller a discretion to retain payments of stamp duty which were not made pursuant to any legal obligation.	None
The absence of any qualification of this kind in Section 111(1) suggests to my mind an obligation to refund the overpaid duty rather than a discretion to withhold repayment in situations which the legislature might have specified but did not.	None
It must be borne in mind that the occasion for the exercise of the authority conferred by Section 111(1) is the finding of an overpayment of stamp duty that is to say, a finding that the Comptroller received moneys to which she had no entitlement.	None
The subsection must be read either as requiring her to refund the overpayment or as conferring a discretion upon her to keep the moneys notwithstanding that she had no entitlement to receive them.	None
The principle that a statute will not be read as authorising expropriation without compensation unless an intention to do so is clearly expressed has been described as a firmly established rule of law.	None
Dawson, J. also expressed the view that the Comptroller did not have a discretion which had to be exercised in accordance with law of restitution.	None
He pointed out that the occasion for the exercise of the authority was identified.	None
The only question which arose was whether the authority must be exercised when the necessary finding of overpayment had been made or whether its exercise was discretionary.	None
Dawson, J. observed that if the common law, rather than the subsection, were to govern the Comptrollers obligation to make a refund, then no doubt a refund would now be required.	None
In fact, this principle is very important to understand the problem raised in this Court.	None
The Central Excise Act provides for every situation for levy, collection and refund of tax.	None
If an overpayment has been made for whatever reason, the amount has to be refunded.	None
The Excise Officer, who deals with an application for refund, has to find out whether an overpayment has been made under the Act.	None
He may, for any reason to be found in the Act, decline to give refund.	None
He cannot travel beyond the Act to find other considerations for withholding the refund.	None
As Dawson, J. pointed out if that was the intention of the legislature, the legislature would have expressly provided for it.	None
Dawson, J. observed However, as I have said, I do not regard Section 111(1) as conferring a discretion.	None
Once the Comptroller found that duty had been overpaid, she was under an obligation to refund it.	None
Since Dawson, J. concluded that Section 111(1) did not confer any discretion to the Comptroller to withhold payment of an unlawful levy, he did not express any final opinion on the question of unjust enrichment and passing on of the overpayment of stamp duty to the insurer in that case.	None
However, Dawson, J. observed The better view would seem to be that it is the unjust enrichment of the payee rather than loss suffered by the payer which should govern entitlement to restitution, but, having regard to the view which I take, it is unnecessary to determine that question in these proceedings.	None
I am also of the view that the Excise Act before its amendment in 1991, in particular Rule 10B and later Section 11B, did not confer any power on the Excise Officer to withhold refund on any ground of unjust enrichment, after being satisfied that overpayment of tax has been made.	None
Moreover, refund is to be claimed within six months from the date of payment of tax which means within six months from removal of the goods from the factory.	None
A company may take a very long time to dispose of its goods after clearance.	None
But a claim for refund has to be made within the short time permitted by the Act.	None
These provisions are indicative of the fact that refund claim has to be made regardless of the sale of the goods.	None
That passing on of the incidence of tax was not relevant consideration is also borne out by subsection (3) of Section 11B as well as subrule (3) of Rule 10B, e.g, if there is dispute as to classification of the goods and the assessee takes resort to filing of an appeal which ends in favour of the assessee, refund will have to be made of the excess amount of tax realised to the assessee without his having to make any claim in that regard.	None
In such a situation, the Assistant Collector of Central Excise is not empowered, before refunding the money, to make an enquiry as to whether the duty has been passed on to the consumers.	None
The concept of passing on of the duty cannot be fitted in the provisions of the Excise Duty Act before its amendment in 1991.	None
As has been repeatedly asserted in a number of cases that in a taxing statute, there is nothing to be added and there is nothing to be taken out and the words must be interpreted as they stand.	None
There is no equity about taxation.	None
To introduce the concept of unjust enrichment in the Act even before its amendment in 1991 is not permissible by any canon of construction.	None
Our attention has not been drawn to any provision of the Act which is concerned about the consumers of the product after they pass out of the factory gate.	None
The rule and the section dealing with the refund do not contain any provision that the Excise Officer will be entitled to withhold refund if it is found that the duty has been passed on to the consumers.	None
As I have stated earlier powers and functions of the Excise Officer are circumscribed by the Act.	None
He cannot take into consideration anything which is not specifically contained in the Act.	None
The contention of Mr Parasaran on behalf of the Union of India has been that the incidence of tax is on the ultimate consumer.	None
As I have pointed out earlier, the Central Excise Act is not at all concerned with the ultimate consumer.	None
Even if it is not possible for a manufacturer to sell the goods, the duty will have to be paid.	None
If it is found after sale of the goods that there is any shortlevy or underlevy, the duty will still have to be paid by the manufacturer.	None
If there is a penalty imposable because of shortlevy or underlevy or any interest is payable, it is the manufacturer who has to bear it.	None
If the goods are lost after production, the manufacturer will have to pay duty on the lost goods.	None
To sum up, under the Central Excises and Salt Act, 1944, there is only one duty and that has been imposed on manufacture.	None
This duty has to be paid before clearance.	None
This duty has to be paid in the manner and mode laid down by the Act.	None
The Act does not impose any other duty.	None
The Act is not concerned with what happens after the goods have been cleared.	None
If the duty has been erroneously imposed, the refund of the duty must be made to the person on whom it is imposed.	None
Refund of tax must not be confused with restitution or compensation.	None
In my judgment, there is only one taxpayer and it is the person who pays the tax at the time of clearance of goods.	None
There is no other tax imposed by the Central Excise Act.	None
How the burden of tax is borne or its economic impact on the manufacturer are not matters within the purview of the Central Excise Act.	None
No notice of these considerations can be taken in deciding the application for refund by the Excise Officer.	None
Article 265 of the Constitution enjoins that no duty shall be levied and collected except in accordance with law.	None
If it is found that a manufacturer has been asked to pay more than what he is liable to pay under the Central Excise Act, he is immediately entitled to get the refund of the wrongfully collected duty.	None
This constitutional guarantee cannot be sidetracked in any manner.	None
Every manufacturer tries to maximise his profits.	None
When he sells goods, he fixes a price at which he can make the maximum profits.	None
Higher prices do not necessarily fetch higher profits.	None
The manufacturer has to sell his products and if the prices are too high, the products will not sell.	None
He has to fix a price keeping in view the costs incurred by him (this will include costs of production as well as selling costs and also the overheads) and also the taxes he has to pay.	None
He will also have to take into consideration the market forces, the effective demand for his products and also the nature and price of the competing products in the market.	None
He will only fix such a price which the traffic can bear.	None
It is wrong to presume that if taxes are raised, the manufacturer has merely to pass on the burden to the consumers by raising the price.	None
It should always be borne in mind that a manufacturer has to generate sufficient income to pay for the prices of inputs, wages to the employees, rents, fuel charges, overheads and many other charges, including direct and indirect taxes.	None
Every type of tax, except only those which are levied on the profits like income tax and surtax on companys profits, will have to be included in the price.	None
The price must be high enough to fetch sufficient income to the manufacturer to pay for all these things and stay in business.	None
If the manufacturer is a company, as the appellant herein is, out of the profits, specific and general reserves will have to be created.	None
Provisions have to be made for known liabilities like provident fund and gratuity for workers, etc.	None
Debentureholders and preferential shareholders will have to be paid.	None
Dividends will also have to be paid to the shareholders who have invested their money in the company.	None
All these things will have to be paid out of the profits made by a company after paying all the expenses including excise and other duties.	None
A manufacturer has also to take into account that all the goods produced by him may not be sold in the year of production itself.	None
That means a large amount of circulating capital will remain blocked.	None
This will also lead to higher interest charges.	None
In fact, there is hardly a company which does not have to carry inventories of taxpaid finished goods year after year.	None
Goods distributed for sale to various outlets may not be sold for months or even years.	None
Such goods may ultimately have to be sold at large discounts or even at a loss.	None
Many products after some time cannot be sold at all for various reasons.	None
In the case of BSC Footwear Ltd. v. Ridgeway 1972 AC 544 the House of Lords dealt with a case of a wellknown shoemanufacturing company.	None
It was found that the unsold stock of shoes of the company at the end of the trading year was generally about a third of the quantity actually sold in that year.	None
Substantial part of the stockinhand at the end of the year would be sold either at reduced prices in January sales and thereafter at even lower prices in later sales.	None
The question in that case was how to value the unsold stock at the end of the trading year.	None
That question does not arise in this case, but it is illustrative of the difficulty of selling goods produced by a manufacturer.	None
Can it be said in such cases when a substantial portion of the goods are being sold at an undervalue and thus causing large erosion of profits, that the incidence of duty has been merrily passed on to the consumers?	None
The goods could not be sold except by reducing the price drastically.	None
It is difficult to say that in such a case incidence of tax is being borne by the consumers and the loss by the producer.	None
BSC Footwear case illustrates the predicament of an average manufacturer.	None
A substantial quantity of taxpaid products cannot be disposed of as a matter of course and the manufacturer has to get rid of the unsold products by organising first sale at a discount thereafter at even lower prices.	None
This is a problem with every manufacturer and to assume that the excise duty can be passed on to the consumer without any corresponding loss to the manufacturer is to ignore reality.	None
In the case of British Paints India Ltd. v. CIT 1978 111 ITR 53 Cal the problem was once again of valuation of unsold stock of a paint manufacturer.	None
It was recognised that paints had a very short shelf life.	None
In other words, unsold cans of paints lying on the shelves of the various outlets of the manufacturer could not retain its quality and utility for indefinite length of time and became unfit for market.	None
In that case, the question was whether the company was entitled to depart from the usual practice of valuing the unsold stock at the end of the year on cost or market price, whichever was lower, basis.	None
The Court said Yes.	None
The Court held that the company was entitled to value its unsold stock of the goods in process on the basis of the cost of raw materials and finished products on the basis of its costs.	None
It was recognised that the company might have to sell a portion of its products ultimately at a vastly reduced price.	None
I have not understood the concept of passing on of tax liability.	None
If this argument is taken to its logical conclusion, then it means that the manufacturing company does not incur any expenditure at all.	None
The taxes as well as the costs of production are recovered through price.	None
Will that mean that a company does not have any cost of production?	None
The wages of labourers, their provident fund, gratuity, bonus, the costs of raw material, the fuel charges, the overheads all these things have to be paid out of the money generated by the company.	None
This can only be done through price obtained by the sale of goods.	None
A suit for short sale by a manufacturing company or recovery of money for overcharging can be defeated by saying that all these things have been passed on to the consumer.	None
An electricity supply company or a coal supplier can also take the plea, faced with an allegation of excessive charge, that in any event the charges have been passed on to the consumers.	None
As I have emphasised earlier that it is not possible to split up the price of a commodity and find out how much is attributable to labour, how much to cost of production and how much to the overheads.	None
That the buyer pays nothing but the price, has been made clear by Section 2(10) and also Section 4 of the Sale of Goods Act.	None
Section 64A permits the seller to add an amount equal to any new tax imposed or any tax increased if such imposition or increment has taken place after the contract was entered into and if a different intention does not appear from the terms of the contract.	None
Incidentally, it should be noted that Lord Goddard, J. took into consideration Section 27 of the Finance (No. 2) Act, 1940 which appears to be similar to Section 64A of our Sale of Goods Act, 1930.	None
Section 64A provides 64A. In contracts of sale, amount of increased or decreased taxes to be added or deducted.(1)	None
Unless different intention appears from the terms of the contract in the event of any tax of the nature described in subsection (2) being imposed, increased, decreased or remitted in respect of any goods after the making of any contract for the sale or purchase of such goods without stipulation as to the payment of tax where tax was not chargeable at the time of the making of the contract, or for the sale or purchase of such goods tax paid where tax was chargeable at that time, (a) if such imposition or increase so takes effect that the decreased tax or increased tax, as the case may be, or any part of such tax is paid or is payable, the seller may add so much to the contract price as will be equivalent to the amount paid or payable in respect of such tax or increase of tax, and he shall be entitled to be paid and to sue for and recover such addition and (b) if such decrease or remission so takes effect that the decreased tax only, or no tax, as the case may be, is paid or is payable, the buyer may deduct so much from the contract price as will be equivalent to the decrease of tax or remitted tax, and he shall not be liable to pay, or be sued for, or in respect of, such deduction.	None
The provisions of subsection (1) apply to the following taxes, namely (a) any duty of customs or excise on goods (b) any tax on the sale or purchase of goods.	None
The English law in this regard is the same.	None
Lord Goddards judgment goes to show that even if the duty element was separately shown in the invoice what the buyer pays is the price of the product and nothing else.	None
The seller similarly gets only the price.	None
Lord Goddard, J. also noted the fact in that case that the burden of the tax had been passed on.	None
This, according to Lord Goddard, J., did not make any difference.	None
In the case of Paprika Ltd. v. Board of Trade 1944 KB 327 a person was called upon to pay penalty which was three times the price at which the articles were expected to be sold.	None
The Divisional Court rejected the argument that the tax element in the price should be excluded because it was no price at all.	None
It was an amount which would ultimately go to the Government.	None
The Court recognised the fact that the price could be affected by the tax element but it does not cease to be the price which buyer has to pay even if the price is expressed to be as X plus purchase tax.	None
This case was cited with approval by Lord Goddard, J. (as His Lordship then was) in the case of Love v. Norman Wright (Builders) Ltd. 1944 1	None
All ER 618 The question before the Court of Appeal was whether the seller of goods under a contract made after the purchase tax had been imposed by law could call upon the purchaser to pay the tax exigible in respect of the sale in addition to the agreed price at which the goods were to be supplied.	None
Goddard, J., pointed out that if a seller quoted a price X plus purchase tax, the buyer must pay the tax as part of the purchase price.	None
Conversely, if a seller agreed to supply goods for a certain sum, then he could not call on the buyer to pay anything extra for tax additionally, unless he was authorised by any statute to do so.	None
In George Oakes (P) Ltd. v. State of Madras AIR 1962 SC 1037 this Court was called upon to consider whether a dealer can pass on his tax liability as such to his customer.	None
In that decision while rejecting the contention that the tax liability as such can be transferred to the buyers, this Court referred to the observations of Lawrence, J. in Paprika Ltd. v. Board of Trade and Goddard, L.J, in Love v. Norman Wright (	None
Builders) Ltd.	None
In the former case, Lawrence, J. observed Whenever a sale attracts purchase tax, that tax presumably affects the price which the seller who is liable to pay the tax demands, but it does not cease to be the price which the buyer has to pay even if the price is expressed as X plus purchase tax.	None
In Love case Goddard, L.J, observed Where an article is taxed, whether by purchase tax, customs duty or excise duty, the tax becomes part of the price which ordinarily the buyer will have to pay.	None
The price of an ounce of tobacco is what it is because of the rate of tax, but on a sale there is only one consideration, though made up of cost plus profit plus tax.	None
So, if a seller offers goods for sale, it is for him to quote a price which includes the tax if he desires to pass it on to the buyer if the buyer agrees to the price it is not for him to consider how it is made up, or whether the seller has included tax or not.	None
In that decision, reference was also made to the decision of this Court in Tata Iron and Steel Co. Ltd. v. State of Bihar 1958 SCR 1355.	None
Therein Das, C.J who delivered the majority judgment of the Court said The circumstance that the 1947 Act, after the amendment, permitted the seller who was a registered dealer to collect the sales tax as a tax from the purchaser does not do away with the primary liability of the seller to pay the sales tax.	None
This is further made clear by the fact that the registered dealer need not, if he so pleases or chooses, collect the tax from the purchaser and sometimes by reason of competition with other registered dealers he may find it profitable to sell his goods and to retain his old customers even at the sacrifice of the sales tax.	None
This also makes it clear that the sales tax need not be passed on to the purchasers and this fact does not alter the real nature of the tax which, by the express provisions of the law, is cast upon the seller.	None
The buyer is under no liability to pay sales tax in addition to the agreed sale price unless the contract specifically provides otherwise.	None
See Love v. Norman Wright	None
(Builders) Ltd.	None
From all these observations, it is clear that when the seller passes on his tax liability to the buyer, the amount recovered by the dealer is really part of the entire consideration paid by the buyer and the distinction between the two amounts  tax and price  loses all significance.	None
These decisions were reaffirmed by this Court in the case of Delhi Cloth and General Mills Co. Ltd. v. CST 1971 2 SCC 559.	None
In the case of Delhi Cloth and General Mills Co. Ltd. v. CST Hegde, J., speaking for the Court, once again emphasised (SCC p. 562, para 7)	None
Unless the price of an article is controlled, it is always open to the buyer and the seller to agree upon the price to be payable.	None
While doing so it is open to the dealer to include in the price the tax payable by him to the Government.	None
If he does so, he cannot be said to be collecting the tax payable by him from his buyers.	None
The levy and collection of tax is regulated by law and not by contract.	None
So long as there is no law empowering the dealer to collect tax from his buyer or seller, there is no legal basis for saying that the dealer is entitled to collect the tax payable by him from his buyer or seller.	None
Whatever collection that may be made by the dealer from his customers the same can only be considered as valuable consideration for the goods sold.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
I have been at great pains to emphasise that if the seller passes on his tax liability to the buyer, the amount equivalent to the tax received by the seller is part of the entire sale consideration.	None
It is not collection of tax, because levy and collection of tax is regulated by law and not by contract.	None
Whatever may have been collected by a seller from his customer on account of tax, the same can only be considered as valuable consideration for the price of the goods sold.	None
What the buyer pays is the price of the goods and not the components of the price.	None
Production costs, selling costs, overheads, taxes, everything goes into fixation of the price.	None
Moreover, the market conditions will have to be taken into account.	None
If the price is too high for the market to bear, the goods will not sell.	None
In order to absorb the excise duty, the manufacturer may have to cut various types of costs.	None
It may have to reduce its profit, pay lesser dividends to shareholders, he may not readily agree to any increment in pay or payment of bonus or other benefits to the workers.	None
It has not been explained how it can be readily assumed that all that the seller has to do to absorb higher duty is to include it in its price and pass it on to the consumers.	None
If the Preamble to the Constitution and social justice are borne in mind, then it may as well be argued, as Karl Marx did, that every article of manufacture is congealed labour.	None
If the labour is given just reward for the work done by him, no surplus value will be left.	None
It is this surplus value extracted from the labour through the pricing mechanism that becomes the manufacturers profit.	None
To prevent unjust enrichment, the entire surplus should go back to the labour.	None
But, here we are not concerned with social and economic theories, but only with the prosaic realm of law as it stands.	Issue
Harold Laski in his wellknown book Introduction to Politics pointed out the difference between role of law and role of politics by saying that the lawyers will have to take the law as it stands.	None
It is not for them to ask why these laws should be our laws?	None
What ends do these laws serve?	None
Why should those ends be our ends?	None
Whereas a student of politics may ask all these questions.	None
Laski said We have to add, so to say, a teleology to law.	None
In this case also we are not entitled to add any teleology to law.	None
We have to take the Central Excise Act as it stands.	None
We may or may not like the law.	None
But for that reason we cannot discard it or its language to bring out an abnormal meaning.	None
If the meaning of price as given in the Sale of Goods Act is borne in mind and its implications as explained in judgments referred to hereinabove are kept in view, then it can never be said that the seller has charged anything but the price of the goods from his buyer.	None
He cannot by a contract call upon the buyer to pay any tax which is the prerogative of a taxing statute.	None
Even if he quotes the price as X (costs) Y (taxes) Z (profit), what the buyer will pay is the price of the goods and nothing else.	None
Neither the costs nor the taxes are passed on to the buyer.	None
Unjust Enrichment	None
The facile assumption that when excise duty is imposed or raised, it can be passed on to the consumer by merely raising the price with no corresponding loss or detriment to the manufacturer has not been made on the basis of any market study.	None
In fact, before the new amendments were effected no indepth study was at all done by the legislature.	None
The basic premise of this line of reasoning is fallacious.	None
The Finance Minister in his budget speech for the year 199495 (206 ITR p. 19) stated Over the years, our indirect tax structure has grown into a complex maze of high and multiple rates, with numerous exemptions, and different rates being applicable for the same product for different uses and users.	None
This has resulted in unnecessary complexity leading to administrative abuse, mounting litigation and uncertain economic impact.	None
All this has effectively eroded the tax base and buoyancy of the system and created serious economic distortions.	None
To illustrate the enormity of excise burden which has to be borne by the manufacturers, it may be mentioned that in the Central Excise Tariff Act, 1985, duty on oils used for skincare was 105 per cent and duty on residual oil which was not specifically mentioned under the Heading 3305.90 was 105 per cent.	None
The duty on paints and varnishes under the Heading 32.09 was as high as 60 per cent.	None
Under the Heading 33.07 preshave, shaving or aftershave preparations had to bear duty of 105 per cent.	None
The examples of high excise duty can be multiplied.	None
It cannot be blindly assumed that levy of excise duty does not cause any financial hardship or loss to the manufacturers because they can merrily pass it on to the consumers.	None
In fact, in very many cases, the Central Government had to issue exemption notifications on the representation made by industries exempting goods wholly or partially from excise duty having regard to the plight to which the industries had been reduced under the impact of taxation.	None
The economic reality that rise in duty causes financial hardship to the manufacturer and that the manufacturer cannot get rid of that hardship by simply passing on the duty has been recognised by the Central Government itself by giving relief to the manufacturers by various exemption notifications.	None
Even in cases where exemption notifications could not be issued retrospectively, an Act was passed to help the manufacturers.	None
The Central Duties of Excise (Retrospective Exemption) Act, 1986 was passed on 891986 to give retrospective effect to certain notifications to enable the Excise authorities to refund duties of excise which had already been collected in certain cases.	None
It was stated by Section 2 of the Act that the Act shall be deemed to have and to have always had, effect on and from the 1st day of March, 1986.	None
It went on to provide The duties of excise which have been collected, but which would not have been so collected if the said notification had been in force at all material times, shall be refunded The duties of excise which have become payable, but which would not have been so payable if the said notification had been in force at all material times, shall not be required to be paid.	None
Any person claiming refund of any duty of excise under subsection (2) may make an application for refund of such duty to the Assistant Collector of Central Excise before the expiry of six months from the commencement of this Act.	Reasoning
It had the effect of refunding the duties of excise which had already been collected and declaring the duties of excise which had become payable (but would not have been payable if the notifications had been in force) shall not be required to be paid.	None
This Act was passed in recognition of the fact that high excise duty causes hardship to the manufacturers.	None
They must be given relief even with retrospective effect.	None
This Act is important for the purpose of this case because it goes to show the legislative intent.	None
The legislature never intended before 1991 that refunds of excise duty will not be given to the manufacturers but to the buyers of the goods.	None
The Central Excise Act is totally silent on this aspect of the matter and we shall not add a rider to the Central Excise Act to deny any refund due to the manufacturer.	None
It has also to be borne in mind that the rates of duty in India are much higher than in USA, Australia or Canada.	None
Its economic impact is much greater.	None
In fact, in the case of United States v. Jefferson Electric Manufacturing Co.	None
the dispute related to levy of excise duty at the rate of 5 per cent.	None
In Air Canada case the disputed duty was 5 cents per gallon.	None
It is needless to speculate how the courts would have reacted if they had to face the high tax regime that exists in India.	None
Mason, C.J in the case of Commr. of State Revenue v. Royal Insurance Australia Ltd. noted how the theory that the burden imposed by higher excise duty can be passed on to the consumers without any economic loss to the manufacturer has been rejected in various courts in the United States, Canada and also Australia.	None
Mason, C.J observed that this economic theory had major difficulties.	None
The first was that to deny recovery when the plaintiff shifted the burden of the imposition of the tax or charge to third parties will often leave a plaintiff who suffered loss or damage without a remedy.	None
Another reason given by Mason, C.J was that an enquiry into and a determination of the loss or damage sustained by a plaintiff who had passed on a tax or charge was a very complex undertaking.	None
Mason, C.J also pointed out that the basis of restitutionary relief was not compensation for loss or damage sustained but restoration to the plaintiff of what has been taken or received from the plaintiff without justification.	None
Mason, C.J in his judgment illustrated the proposition with a number of cases to show that the doctrine of passingon was fraught with many difficulties.	None
An American case was cited where the Supreme Court of U.S had rejected the doctrine of passingon under antitrust laws where the plaintiffs had passed on overpayments to their customers (Hanover Shoe Inc. v. United Shoe Machinery Corp. 392 US 481) Commenting on this, Mason, C.J observed that though the context is different, the reasons given for the rejection were relevant for the present case.	None
They include the difficulty of determining the economic impact upon the plaintiffs business of passing on the overpayment, the practical problems which availability of the defence would generate involving massive evidence and complicated theories.	None
Further the defence would probably apply all the way down the chain of distribution to the ultimate consumer who would have little interest to sue.	None
The U.S Supreme Court also noted that economic theories rely upon the assumptions which do not operate in the real world, thereby making the proof of passing on extremely difficult.	None
This view was also expressed in the opinion of the Advocate General in Amministrazione delle Finanze dello Stato v. San Giorgio SpA.	None
Mason, C.J concluded that The United States and European decisions demonstrate that any acceptance of the defence of passing on is fraught with both practical and theoretical difficulties.	None
Indeed, the difficulties are so great that, in my view, the defence should not succeed unless it is established that the defendants enrichment is not at the expense of the plaintiff but at the expense of some other person or persons.	None
In view of all these, I see no basis to deny the refund to a manufacturer on the facile assumption that burden of duty has been passed on to the consumers without any loss or detriment to the manufacturer.	None
The absurdity of this doctrine of passingon can well be demonstrated by the following examples.	None
Supposing, a manufacturer of pulp sells his product to a rayon manufacturer which uses the pulp to manufacture rayon it can be said that the burden of duty has been passed on to the rayon manufacturer.	None
The rayon manufacturer, in his turn, includes the cumduty price in his costs and includes it in his price when he sells his yarn to a cloth manufacturer.	None
The cloth manufacturer in his turn will include the dutypaid price of rayon in his costs and will sell his products to a garment manufacturer at dutypaid price.	None
The garmentmaker will sell the garments to the actual users.	None
Can the last consumer establish that he has borne the incidence of an illegal excise duty imposed on pulp and claim refund of the unlawful duty on pulp?	None
Can he at all be made aware of such an unlawful levy on pulp?	None
Or will it be that the rayon manufacturer will get the refund as a consumer of pulp even though he has included the dutypaid price in his costs of raw material for production of rayon and has thereby passed on the burden to his customers?	None
These illustrations can be multiplied ad infinitum.	None
If a scrap dealer buys dutypaid scrap and sells to a carparts manufacturer who in his turn treats such price as his cost and includes it in his price (duty included) and sells the parts to a car manufacturer, who in his turn sells cars to the actual users, who will get back any illegal levy of excise duty on scraps?	None
This problem has other dimensions.	None
The Excise Act cannot be viewed in isolation.	None
If there is an illegal levy of paper and a lawyer buys paper at cumduty price, he gets deduction of the entire sum in computation of income under the Income Tax Act.	None
Can he claim refund of excise duty as being the ultimate consumer?	None
As I said earlier, these are not isolated examples, but things that are happening in everyday life.	None
Dutypaid price charged by a manufacturer is his income for income tax purposes, turnover for sales tax and turnover tax.	None
It has a variety of other fiscal dimensions.	None
How can it ever be assumed that an illegal levy of tax will be a source of joy for the taxpayer?	None
He will happily pass on the burden and merrily enjoy the refund.	None
The argument by reference to the Directive Principles that unlawfully collected tax must be retained by the Government for the common good of the people and also to involve the weaker sections of the people may have a populist appeal, but is without any basis having regard to the provisions of the Central Excise Act as well as the Excise Tariff Act.	None
The Central Excise Act levies a tax on manufacture of goods.	None
Very often goods are manufactured by smallscale industries or individuals for the benefit of large industries.	None
If a smallscale paper pulp manufacturer who struggles to exist, cannot get back an illegal levy of excise duty because the consumer, a largescale viscose fibre manufacturer, has ultimately borne the burden of the duty and the illegally collected duty is paid back to that large company, the weaker section far from being benefited, will be thoroughly robbed.	None
In fact, if we look at the Central Excise Tariff Act, it will be seen that the vast majority of the products are not for household use or for the common man.	None
The list of excisable commodities starts with animal products, which may include products of the kind unfit or unsuitable for human consumption guts, bladders or stomachs of animals or animal blood or animal fat, other than pig fat (Chapter 2).	None
Obviously these have industrial uses, but a common man will not buy them.	None
Likewise, Lac, Gums, Resins (Chapter 13), Bituminous and Asphalt, Chemical Compound (Chapter 27), Chemical Compounds  Organic and Inorganic (Chapter 28), Explosives, Pyrotechnic Products Pyrophoric Alloys and other Combustible Preparations (Chapter 36) will only be used by large industries.	None
A large number of chemical products are taxed under the heading Miscellaneous Chemical Products, like Graphite, Activated Carbon, Rubber Accelerators, compound plasticisers, organic composite solvents (Chapter 38), charged fire extinguishing grenade are not used by the common man.	None
In fact, the Schedule to the Central Excise Tariff Act has as many as 96 Chapters and appears to contain more entries relating to goods which are used by trade and industry than the common man in everyday life like Base Metals, Iron and Steel, Aluminium Metal (Chapter 72), Nuclear Reactors, Boilers, machineries, mechanical appliances parts thereof, electric motors and generators, rotary converters, transformers, static converters, electromagnets, etc. (Chapter 85).	None
The Schedule also includes Railway or Tramway Locomotives, RollingStock and parts thereof Railway or Tramway Track Fixtures and Fittings and parts thereof Mechanical Traffic Signalling Equipment of all kinds (Chapter 86).	None
This entry is followed by vehicles other than Railway or Tramway etc.	None
(Chapter 87).	None
This entry includes motor cars, motor vehicles, tanks and other armoured fighting vehicles and also parts and accessories of the motor cars and motor vehicles principally designed for transport of persons, motor vehicles for the transport of goods.	None
Even here it should be noted that, having regard to the price of the motor cars and motor vehicles, it is not the weaker section of the population who uses these vehicles.	None
In the name of benefiting the weaker section, unlawfully and illegally levied duty of excise on parts and accessories and various inputs manufactured by small manufacturers for use of the large manufacturers will not be returned to them but handed over to the large manufacturer or rich consumers who have the resource and ability to claim it.	None
There are of course household goods or goods of everyday necessity like edible oil, toothpaste, toothbrush, soap, some textile articles and possibly some items falling under paper and paper board are used by the common man in everyday life.	None
But taking an overall view of the tariff items in the Schedule to the Central Excise Tariff Act, it can hardly be said that excise duty by and large is on goods to be used by the common man.	None
Moreover, there are many industries reserved for smallscale sector.	None
This has been done to protect smallscale industries from competition from the big manufacturers.	None
If for example, a manufacturer of wristwatch strap (reserved for small sector) is unable to get back any illegally imposed duty of excise because the watch straps have been sold to a large watchmanufacturing company and that large company is given the refund, the weaker section will not benefit in any way.	None
Even for the consumer goods, it is not in the realm of belief that an ordinary buyer will be able to chase the Excise Officer and claim refund of duty illegally imposed on the manufacturer.	None
For example, a person buying toothbrush from the local grocery shop, will not retain the cash memo for years and years and even if he does so, he will not know that there is a dispute about the levy of excise duty pending.	None
Furthermore, a man who purchases a toothbrush in Madras will not be able to claim refund of duty from the proper Excise Officer who has jurisdiction over the company at Bombay.	None
We shall bear all these considerations in mind before trying to interpret the law in a way which will benefit the weaker sections of the people and give them a sense of participation in the development of the country.	None
Moreover, only the manufacturer has to separately show the duty element in his invoice.	None
The wholesaler, the distributor or the retailer has no such obligation.	None
Ordinary customers buy their goods at the retail outlet, where even if a cash memo is given, the duty element will not be shown separately.	None
How will the common man know that he has paid any duty and if so of what amount?	None
In my view, the entire argument based on unjust enrichment is founded on a false premise.	None
It will be wrong to assume that the duty element can be included in the price and that no prejudice will be caused to the manufacturer by the levy or enhancement of the duty.	None
To take this position is to ignore the economic realities.	None
There may also be a situation when a manufacturer will not be able to certify that he has not passed on the duty even though he has borne it.	None
Supposing a manufacturer is charging Rs 100 per unit of goods.	None
The price of Rs 100 is calculated on the basis of Rs 80 as costs, Rs 10 as profits and Rs 10 as excise duty.	None
The excise duty element is enhanced unlawfully by Rs 5.	None
In such a case, the manufacturer may either raise the price of the goods by Rs 5 or he may decide to reduce his profit to Rs 5 and sell the goods at the same price.	None
In the second case when the manufacturer reduces the profit element to Rs 5 and sells the goods at Rs 100, can it be said that he has passed on the burden of excise duty to his customers.	None
The price is inclusive of the duty element.	None
In a sense, the burden of duty borne by the manufacturer has been passed on.	None
But then again, the manufacturer has suffered diminution of profit.	None
Can it be said in such a case that if the manufacturer manages to get an order of refund of duty, it will be unethical for him to get the amount because this will be unlawful enrichment?	None
The manufacturer in a case like this will not be in a position to certify that the burden of duty has not been included in the price of the goods but the fact remains that in order to maintain the price of goods at the optimum level the manufacturer had to suffer loss of profit.	None
The Central Government has been empowered to exempt, generally or absolutely by notification, excisable goods from the whole or any part of the duty imposed thereon.	None
Judicial notice must be taken that in very many cases, having regard to the hardship suffered by the industry and representations made by the industry, duties have been reduced or exempted by issuing appropriate notifications or even by legislation.	None
Scope of Sections 11B, 11D, 12A, 12B, 12C and 12D of the Central Excises and Salt Act, 1944 Sections 11B and 11D in Chapter II and Sections 12A, 12B, 12C and 12D in Chapter IIA are now to be considered 11B. Claim for refund of duty.(1)	Issue
Any person claiming refund of any duty of excise may make an application for refund of such duty to the Assistant Collector of Central Excise before the expiry of six months from the relevant date in such form and manner as may be prescribed and the application shall be accompanied by such documentary or other evidence (including the documents referred to in Section 12A) as the applicant may furnish to establish that the amount of duty of excise in relation to which such refund is claimed was collected from or paid by him and the incidence of such duty has not been passed on by him to any other person Provided that where an application for refund has been made before the commencement of the Central Excises and Customs Laws (Amendment) Act, 1991, such application shall be deemed to have been made under this subsection as amended by the said Act and the same shall be dealt with in accordance with the provisions of subsection (2) as substituted by that Act Provided further that the limitation of six months shall not apply where any duty has been paid under protest.	Reasoning
If, on receipt of any such application, the Assistant Collector of Central Excise is satisfied that the whole or any part of the duty of excise paid by the applicant is refundable, he may make an order accordingly and the amount so determined shall be credited to the Fund Provided that the amount of duty of excise as determined by the Assistant Collector of Central Excise under the foregoing provisions of this subsection shall, instead of being credited to the Fund, be paid to the applicant, if such amount is relatable to (a) rebate of duty of excise on excisable goods exported out of India or on excisable materials used in the manufacture of goods which are exported out of India (b) unspent advance deposits lying in balance in the applicants account current maintained with the Collector of Central Excise (c) refund of credit of duty paid on excisable goods used as inputs in accordance with the rules made, or any notification issued, under this Act (d) the duty of excise paid by the manufacturer, if he had not passed on the incidence of such duty to any other person (e) the duty of excise borne by the buyer, if he had not passed on the incidence of such duty to any other person (f) the duty of excise borne by any other such class of applicants as the Central Government may, by notification in the Official Gazette, specify Provided further that no notification under clause (f) of the first proviso shall be issued unless in the opinion of the Central Government, the incidence of duty has not been passed on by the persons concerned to any other person.	None
Notwithstanding anything to the contrary contained in any judgment, decree, order or direction of the Appellate Tribunal or any court or in any other provision of this Act or the rules made thereunder or any other law for the time being in force, no refund shall be made except as provided in subsection (2).	None
Every notification under clause (f) of the first proviso to subsection (2) shall be laid before each House of Parliament, if it is sitting, as soon as may be after the issue of the notification, and, if it is not sitting, within seven days of its reassembly, and the Central Government shall seek the approval of Parliament to the notification by a resolution moved within a period of fifteen days beginning with the day on which the notification is so laid before the House of the People and if Parliament makes any modification in the notification or directs that the notification should cease to have effect, the notification shall thereafter have effect only in such modified form or be of no effect, as the case may be, but without prejudice to the validity of anything previously done thereunder.	None
For the removal of doubts, it is hereby declared that any notification issued under clause (f) of the first proviso to subsection (2), including any such notification approved or modified under subsection (4), may be rescinded by the Central Government at any time by notification in the Official Gazette.	None
Explanation.	None
For the purposes of this section, (A) refund includes rebate of duty of excise on excisable goods exported out of India or on excisable materials used in the manufacture of goods which are exported out of India (B) relevant date means, (a) in the case of goods exported out of India where a refund of excise duty paid is available in respect of the goods themselves or, as the case may be, the excisable materials used in the manufacture of such goods, if the goods are exported by sea or air, the date on which the ship or the aircraft in which such goods are loaded, leaves India, or if the goods are exported by land, the date on which such goods pass the frontier, or if the goods are exported by post, the date of despatch of goods by the Post Office concerned to a place outside India (b) in the case of goods returned for being remade, refined, reconditioned, or subjected to any other similar process, in any factory, the date of entry into the factory for the purposes aforesaid (c) in the case of goods to which banderols are required to be affixed if removed for home consumption but not so required which exported outside India, if returned to a factory after having been removed from such factory for export out of India, the date of entry into the factory (d) in a case where a manufacturer is required to pay a sum, for a certain period, on the basis of the rate fixed by the Central Government by notification in the Official Gazette in full discharge of his liability for the duty leviable on his production of certain goods, if after the manufacturer has made the payment on the basis of such rate for any period but before the expiry of that period such rate is reduced, the date of such reduction (e) in the case of a person, other than the manufacturer, the date of purchase of the goods by such person (ea) in the case of goods which are exempt from payment of duty by a special order issued under subsection (2) of Section 5A, the date of issue of such order (f) in any other case, the date of payment of duty.	None
11D. Duties of excise collected from the buyer to be deposited with the Central Government.(1)	None
Notwithstanding anything to the contrary contained in any order or direction of the Appellate Tribunal or any court or in any other provision of this Act or the rules made thereunder, every person who has collected any amount from the buyer of any goods in any manner as representing duty of excise, shall forthwith pay the amount so collected to the credit of the Central Government.	None
The amount paid to the credit of the Central Government under subsection (1) shall be adjusted against the duty of excise payable by the person on finalisation of assessment and where any surplus is left after such adjustment, the amount of such surplus shall either be credited to the Fund or, as the case may be, refunded to the person who has borne the incidence of such amount, in accordance with the provisions of Section 11B and the relevant date for making an application under that section in such cases shall be the date of the public notice to be issued by the Assistant Collector of Central Excise.	None
12A. Price of goods to indicate the amount of duty paid thereon.	None
Notwithstanding anything contained in this Act or any other law for the time being in force, every person who is liable to pay duty of excise on any goods shall, at the time of clearance of the goods, prominently indicate in all the documents relating to assessment, sales invoice, and other like documents, the amount of such duty which will form part of the price at which such goods are to be sold.	None
12B. Presumption that incidence of duty has been passed on to the buyer.	None
Every person who has paid the duty of excise on any goods under this Act shall, unless the contrary is proved by him, be deemed to have passed on the full incidence of such duty to the buyer of such goods.	None
12C. Consumer Welfare Fund.(1)	None
There shall be established by the Central Government a fund, to be called the Consumer Welfare Fund.	None
There shall be credited to the Fund, in such manner as may be prescribed, (a) the amount of duty of excise referred to in subsection (2) of Section 11B or subsection (2) of Section 11C or subsection (2) of Section 11D (b) the amount of duty of customs referred to in subsection (2) of Section 27 or subsection (2) of Section 28A, or subsection (2) of Section 28B of the Customs Act, 1962 (52 of 1962) (c) any income from investment of the amount credited to the Fund and any other monies received by the Central Government for the purposes of this Fund.	None
12D. Utilisation of the Fund.(1)	None
Any money credited to the Fund shall be utilised by the Central Government for the welfare of the consumers in accordance with such rules as that Government may make in this behalf.	None
The Central Government shall maintain or, if it thinks fit, specify the authority which shall maintain, proper and separate account and other relevant records in relation to the Fund in such form as may be prescribed in consultation with the Comptroller and Auditor General of India.	None
Section 11B(1) contemplates that for claiming refund of any duty of excise a person has to apply with documentary evidence to establish, (1) the amount of duty of excise was collected from him or paid by him and (2) the incidence of such duty has not been passed on by him to any other person.	None
subsection (2) of Section 11B provides that if the Excise Officer is satisfied that the whole or any part of the duty of excise paid by the applicant is refundable, he may make an order accordingly.	None
The refundable amount, however, will be credited to a Fund.	None
The proviso lays down certain circumstances under which the duty may be paid to the applicant.	None
Clause (d) of the proviso says that the duty of excise paid by the manufacturer, if he had not passed on the incidence of such duty to any other person, will be refunded to him.	Decision
These provisions are not in consonance with the charging provisions of the Excise Act and the Rules.	None
The wellknown principle of fiscal legislation is that the charge lies where it falls.	None
It cannot be shifted by a contract.	None
Acts relating to income tax, wealth tax, sales tax as well as excise duty have charging sections.	None
A man may contract with somebody to pay his income tax, a seller may contract with somebody else to pay his sales tax and a manufacturer may contract with a third party to pay the duty of excise.	None
These contracts are not enforceable by or against the Revenue.	None
The Central Excise Act imposes a tax on manufacture.	None
This tax has to be paid before the goods are cleared in the manner laid down by the Act and the Rules.	None
There is no other duty of excise payable under the Act.	None
I have referred to various decisions wherein it has been pointed out that the contract between the manufacturer and a buyer is of no consequence in the matter of payment and collection of excise duty.	None
The question of passing on can only arise after the duty has been fully paid.	None
The duty of excise is never borne by the buyer as stated in clause (e) of the proviso.	None
The buyer may pay a sum equivalent to the duty of excise pursuant to a contract with the manufacturer, but that is a matter of contract.	None
The duty imposed on and collected from the manufacturer, if it is found to be in excess of the charge imposed by Section 3, has to be returned to the manufacturer and nobody else, otherwise charging provision, rules for computation of charge and imposition and collection of duty will become meaningless.	None
If any amount has been realised by the Excise Officer in excess of the charge imposed by the charging section, then such collection is beyond the competence of the Act and also violates Article 265 of the Constitution.	None
It was pointed out in the case of CCE v. National Tobacco Co. of India Ltd.	None
SCC at p. 572 that Article 265 of the Constitution makes a distinction between levy and collection.	None
Levy may include both imposition of a tax as well as assessment.	None
Collection will be recovery of tax.	None
If it is found that a taxpayer has been levied more than the permissible limit imposed by the charging section read with the Excise Tariff Act and the Rules, the levy is bad.	None
The collection pursuant to this levy is equally bad.	None
Such levy and collection are dehors the provisions of the excise act.	None
There is no way that the Central Excise authority can retain the amount or use the amount.	None
In any way it has to refund the amount to the person from whom it has been unlawfully collected by the Excise Officer.	None
According to the Central Excise Act, as Hegde, J. pointed out in the case of Delhi Cloth and General Mills, duty is imposed by a statute whereas the cumduty price is paid by the purchaser under a contract with the manufacturer.	None
No portion of the cumduty price in law can be treated as the duty of excise.	None
Nothing which is not imposed by Section 3 and collected under the provisions of the Excise Act and Rules, can be called duty of excise.	None
In my view this is the basic principle of any tax law.	None
If by any device any amount which is not leviable in law has been levied and collected from a taxpayer, then retention of such amount will be unlawful.	None
Any provision appearing or trying to bar recovery of illegally collected tax is violative of Article 265 of the Constitution and must be struck down as the Barring Act was struck down by the Privy Council in the case of Commr.	None
for Motor Transport v.	None
Antill Ranger Co.	None
Pty. Ltd.	None
If the realisation of tax is in excess of the charge imposed by the Excise Act read with the Excise Tariff Act and Rules, then such levy of tax is not authorised by law.	None
The collection of such excess unlawful levy is also invalid.	None
As the Judicial Committee pointed out if the levy is invalid as an offence against Section 92, it is equally an offence to deny the right to recover it after it has been unlawfully exacted.	None
Therefore, in my view, once it is established that more than what is payable under the statute has been collected from the taxpayer, the taxpayer automatically gets a right to get back the whole amount.	None
If the right is sought to be effectively taken away by imposing conditions, then the law imposing these conditions must be declared to be bad and ultra vires the Constitution.	None
There is another aspect of this matter.	None
The Excise Officer cannot tax more than what is permitted by the statute.	None
If the levy is in excess of the statute, then its retention by the State is unauthorised by law.	None
What is being retained is not in enforcement of the charging section but something else.	None
Such illegally collected tax is not the property of the State and is not within the disposing power of the State.	None
If the money has to be utilised by the State, the State has to find out some legitimacy for having possession of the money.	None
In the Canadian case of Air Canada v. British Columbia retroactive amendment of the Gasoline Tax Act was passed with a new definition of purchaser to make a levy valid and retain the illegally collected amount by setting off against the claim raised by the amended Act.	None
That is the only way in which La Forest, J. could justify, what was otherwise a confiscatory provision.	None
In this case, there has been no attempt to give legitimacy to the holding of the amount or utilisation of the amount by the Government.	None
The entire amount was collected unlawfully.	None
The original sin has not been cured as in Canada by a retroactive charge.	None
I shall now examine the other provisions of the newlyadded sections.	None
Subsection (1) of Section 11B requires an application for refund to be made.	None
Subsection (2) requires the Assistant Commissioner to pass an order of refund provided the conditions set out therein are fulfilled.	None
Subsection (3) merely lays down that no refund shall be made except as provided in subsection (2).	None
There is a non obstante clause that this will operate notwithstanding anything to the contrary contained in any judgment, decree, order etc.	None
It is obvious that new provisions will apply in cases where applications for refund were made before the new provisions came into force and also subsequently.	None
Subsection (3) has no retrospective effect.	None
When a case has been finally heard and disposed of and no application for refund need be made, subsection (3) cannot apply.	None
If there is a judgment, decree or order which has to be carried out, the legislature cannot take away the force and effect of that judgment, decree or order, except by amending the law retrospectively on the basis of which the judgment was pronounced.	None
I have indicated earlier in the judgment and shall not repeat that it is practically impossible for an ultimate consumer to make an application for refund under Section	None
11B. He has to know that there is a dispute about levy of excess duty which is going on between the manufacturer and the Excise authority.	None
He has to know the outcome of that dispute.	None
He has also to find out what is the amount of duty he has borne.	None
This is a difficult process because the ultimate consumer may have a cash memo from his retail seller.	None
A retail seller usually does not give the breakup of duty in the price he charges.	None
The new law requires a manufacturer at the time of clearance of the goods to prominently indicate in the invoice and other documents the amount of such duty which will form part of the price.	None
There is no such requirement for the dealers down the line.	None
It is incomprehensible how a person who buys a cake of soap will know the duty content in the price and whether the excise duty levied was valid or not and how will he find out which is the proper officer, to whom to make an application in the prescribed form for refund of duty and what sort of evidence will he be having in his possession to authenticate his claim.	None
It is rightly contended by Mr Nariman that all these provisions are only an eyewash to retain the illegally exacted excess levy by the Government which is, as a matter of fact what is actually being done.	None
Now I shall deal with Section 11D. Excise duty is levied by the charging Section 3.	None
It has to be paid according to the Excise Tariff Act, 1985 and the Rules.	None
Before clearance of the goods, the assessee is required by Rule 173B to file what is known as price classification list in which full particulars of the goods manufactured and intended to be removed from his factory has to be given.	None
The chapter heading and subheading number under which the goods are to be assessed under the Tariff Act has also to be indicated.	None
The assessee has also to state the rate of duty leviable on each such goods.	None
On the basis of the declaration made by the assessee, the Excise Officer has to make his calculation of duty.	None
For the purpose of proper valuation of the goods assessable ad valorem, pro forma price list for commodities has to be filed.	None
The value of the goods have to be calculated by making deductions from the wholesale price in accordance with Section 4(4) of the Excise Act.	None
There may be dispute as to the valuation or rate of duty for which an adjudication proceeding may have to be taken.	None
But without the approval of the Excise Officer, no goods can be removed from the factory.	None
The assessee has also to maintain an account current.	None
This is laid down by Rule 173G 173G. Procedure to be followed by the assessee.(1)	None
Every assessee shall keep an account current with the Commissioner separately for each excisable goods , in such form and manner as the Commissioner may require, of the duties payable on the excisable goods and in particular such account shall be maintained in triplicate by using indelible pencil and doublesided carbon, and the assessee shall periodically make credit in such account current, by cash payment into the treasury so as to keep the balance, in such account current, sufficient to cover the duty due on the goods intended to be removed at any time and every such assessee shall pay the duty determined by him for each consignment by debit to such account current before removal of the goods This Rule requires advance payment of tax.	None
Money has to be deposited in the treasury well in advance before removal of the goods.	None
Section 11D is a curious piece of legislation.	None
Even after the full amount of duty has been paid and goods have been cleared, the manufacturer is being called upon to deposit with the Central Government any amount collected from the buyer representing duty of excise.	None
In other words, having paid the full amount of duty of excise, the manufacturer is being called upon to deposit the duty element in the price of his goods to be deposited to the credit of the Central Government.	None
The only justification for this appears to be that the entire amount will be held till finalisation of the assessment.	None
But the section provides that if there is any surplus left after such adjustment, the surplus shall not come back to the seller but will be credited to the Fund or paid to the person who has borne the incidence of the duty in accordance with the provisions of Section 11B which means the ultimate consumer.	None
An attempt has been made to salvage this section by construing that this section will apply only if duty has not been paid on the goods or if any excess collection has been made over and above the duty already paid.	None
It is very difficult to agree to such a construction.	None
There cannot be a blanket statutory direction to pay everything collected from a buyer on account of excise duty to be paid over to the Excise Officer.	None
If it is in the nature of advance tax, there has to be some attempt to fix a percentage which needs to be handed over.	None
Otherwise, it will be unreasonable restriction on trade.	None
The sale price is a part of the circulating capital.	None
Goods are converted into money and money is again utilised to manufacture goods.	None
If a substantial portion of this money is taken away without having regard to the actual or probable necessity for the collection, it will be unreasonable restraint on the right of a person to carry on business.	None
Moreover, the amount may be kept till finalisation of assessment.	None
The assessment may not be finalised till the dispute has been decided finally by CEGAT or even by this Court.	None
Will the money be blocked up till then?	None
Supposing the assessee succeeds, why will he not get back the money with interest?	None
This provision has to be contrasted with the advance tax collected under the Income Tax Act.	None
Such collection is authorised by the charging section of the Act Section 4(2) because otherwise, the collection would have gone beyond the scope of the charge.	None
The rate on which the tax is to be collected and the basis is clearly stated.	None
High rates of interest are payable both by the assessee and the Government in appropriate cases.	None
But if an amount is taken in advance, then the residue after adjustment of tax must go back to the taxpayer.	None
That is not the scheme here.	None
So, this cannot be treated as something in the nature of advance collection of tax where duty has not at all been paid on the goods.	None
The second point that this has been done to safeguard against any excess collection from the consumer is equally unreasonable.	None
The excise duty is a duty on the manufacture of the goods.	None
Once full amount of duty has been collected, the Excise authority cannot control any contract between the purchaser and the seller.	None
The Excise Act imposes a charge on the manufacturer.	None
There is no charge of duty levied by the Excise Act on excess collection by the manufacturer from the buyer.	None
Any question of excess collection by the manufacturer from the buyer is entirely out of the purview of the charging section.	None
If the assessee has collected on account of excise duty from the purchaser more than what he has paid, perhaps, a purchaser can bring an action against the seller.	None
In the event of a contractual dispute between the purchaser and the seller, the relevant statutes will be the Contract Act, the Sale of Goods Act and similar other statutes.	None
But the Central Excise Officer cannot under any circumstances, lay his hands on anything more than what is actually levied by the Act.	None
He cannot collect something which is not payable under the charging section even for the purpose of directing it to the Fund or to the actual consumer.	None
The entire Section 11D is ultra vires the charge levied by the Excise Act itself.	None
Moreover, the entire sale price (duty included), will form part of the sales turnover of the assessee on which sales tax will have to be paid under the State Acts.	None
Turnover tax will have to be paid by big assessees.	None
The purchaser may also have to pay purchase tax on the purchase price.	None
In such cases, how will the State Revenue authorities determine the quantum of turnover of sales or purchase for levy of sales tax or purchase tax?	None
The sale proceeds will be income of the assessee for the purpose of levy of income tax.	None
Unlike the Income Tax Act, the assessee has not been given any option to show that he is not liable to pay the amount which is being taken away from his proceeds.	None
He has no opportunity of getting a hearing on this issue.	None
The Income Tax Act enables the assessee, in such circumstances, to dispute the estimation of advance tax made by the Income Tax Officer and file his own estimate (of course at his own peril).	None
Here he has no option but to pay without any hearing.	None
I repeat that a manufacturer cannot be called upon to pay anything except the duty imposed by the charging provisions.	None
Even if the final assessment has not been made, goods may be allowed to be cleared by paying the admitted amount of duty and furnishing the security for the disputed amount.	None
The security may be keeping sufficient money in the account current with the Excise Department or even by furnishing a bond or a bank guarantee.	None
This is provided by the Rules.	None
There is no legal or rational basis for a blanket provision to deposit whatever is included on account of excise duty in the price of the goods sold.	None
The position gets curiouser after the deposit.	None
After adjustment of the tax against the deposit, the surplus amount is not returned to the manufacturer.	None
It has to be credited to the Fund or paid to the person who has borne the incidence of tax, i.e, the ultimate consumer.	None
In other words, the manufacturer will be robbed of a portion of his sale price for no rhyme or reason.	None
This may also have the effect of nullifying the sale contract entered into by the manufacturer with the buyer.	None
The buyer had agreed to pay an agreed price which may include the duty element.	None
The seller agreed to sell the goods to the buyer at that price.	None
Section 64A of the Sale of Goods Act protects the interests of both.	None
How can a portion of that price be taken away and credited to a Fund or paid to the ultimate consumer?	None
What will happen to the contract?	None
The only effect of Section 11D is to rob the manufacturer of a portion of his legitimate dues.	None
These provisions are not in aid of the charge on manufacture levied by the Central Excise Act, but are in excess of the charge and are confiscatory in nature and have to be struck down.	None
It appears to me that by these newly amended provisions, the legislature has merely created a device or a cloak to confiscate the property of the taxpayer.	None
In such a situation, a Bench of five Judges of this Court in Raja Jagannath Baksh Singh v. State of U.P AIR 1962 SC 1563 said that the law has to be struck down as passed in colourable exercise of the power of taxation.	None
It was observed by Gajendragadkar, J., speaking for the Bench the conclusion that a taxing statute is colourable would not and cannot normally be raised merely on the finding that the tax imposed by it is unreasonably high or heavy, because the reasonableness of the extent of the levy is always a matter within the competence of the legislature.	None
Such a conclusion can be reached where in passing the Act, the legislature has merely adopted a device and a cloak to confiscate the property of the citizen taxed.	None
If, however, such a conclusion is reached on the consideration of all relevant facts, that is a separate and independent ground for striking down the Act.	None
So far as sections 12a and 12b are concerned, only thing that has to be pointed out is that these two sections do not change the character of the price of the goods.	None
Both these elements were taken into consideration by Lord Goddard, J. in the case of Love v. Norman Wright (	None
Builders) Ltd.	None
It was stated that even if the burden of duty was passed on and the price was expressed as Pound X plus duty, even then what the buyer paid was the price of the goods and not the duty and the seller obtained the price and nothing else.	None
This principle was reaffirmed time and again, as we have noted earlier in the judgment, in a number of cases by this Court.	None
Apart from what has been stated hereinabove, I find that the entire group of these sections is dehors the charging section of the Central Excise Act.	None
The Central Excise Act imposes a duty on manufacture of goods.	None
Various provisions have been made for computation and collection of that duty.	None
Anything collected in excess of that charge is unlawful.	None
If any provision is made for retention of duties collected without any authority of law, then such provision will be beyond the scope of the charge.	None
It will amount to collecting and retaining something which is not at all dutypayable under Section 3.	None
The legislature has now authorised the Excise Department to retain the illegal levy.	None
In my judgment, these provisions are ultra vires the charge levied by Section 3 and cannot be sustained in any way.	None
In the language of Lord MacMillan in Ayrshire Employers Mutual Insurance Assn. Ltd. v. IRC 27 Tax Cases 331 Tax Cases at p. 337, the legislature has missed fire.	None
The scope of charge in a taxing Act is of the highest importance.	None
Nothing can be realised under a taxing Act beyond that.	None
The new provisions of the excise act are not in aid of the charge imposed by Section 3.	None
These sections are designed to enable the Excise Department to retain what was collected over and above the charge.	None
