The amounts collected in excess of what is actually payable under the charging section is not excise duty at all.	None
Nothing can be collected under a taxing Act which is not authorised by the charging section read with the machinery provisions.	None
The new provisions not only effectively bar recovery of unlawful levies by the taxpayer but have also taken away from him a portion of the price at which he has contracted to sell the goods to the purchasers.	None
How can a portion of the sale price be taken away and retained by the Excise Officer or returned to the buyer in derogation of a contract of sale passes comprehension.	None
I have already noted earlier in the judgment the impossibility of finding out on whom the incidence of charge falls and also the various unworkable problems created by these illconceived amendments.	None
In my view, the amended provisions must be struck down as violative of Article 265 and the guarantee contained in Article 19(1)(g) of the Constitution of India.	None
I am further of the view that the legislature has merely adopted a device and a cloak to confiscate the property of the taxpayer by not only withholding repayment of unlawfully gathered tax but also taking away a portion of the sale price collected from the buyer without any lawful demand or excuse.	None
Every person has a right to contract and bargain for the price.	None
Section 11D places unreasonable fetter to the freedom to carry on trade and commerce and violates the guarantee given by Article 19(1)(g) of the Constitution.	None
Various other points were raised in these cases.	Decision
I am not dealing with them separately, but I express my respectful concurrence with the views of my learned Brother Paripoornan, J. that an action by way of a suit or writ petition will be maintainable, depending upon the facts and circumstances of the case.	Decision
I am entirely in agreement with the views expressed by him and the reasoning thereof on Points E, F and G of the concluding part of his judgment.	None
In conclusion, I hold that the Government is permitted to levy and retain only that much of excise duty which can be lawfully levied and collected under the Central Excise Act read with the Central Excise Tariff Act, 1985 and the Central Excise Rules and various notifications issued from time to time.	None
Anything collected beyond this is unlawful and cannot be retained by the Government under any pretext.	None
The illegal levy and collection of duty violates not only the Central Excise Act and the Rules but also offends Article 265 of the Constitution of India.	None
I am of the view that the provision of Section 11B is a device for denying the claim for refund of duty to a taxpayer and must be struck down as violative of Article 265 of the Constitution.	None
It in effect tries to perpetuate an illegal levy without altering the basis of the law under which the levy was made in any way.	None
It is also a colourable piece of legislation and must be struck down.	None
Section 11D imposes unreasonable restriction on the right to carry trade and violates Article 19(1)(g).	None
Excise authority cannot deny the manufacturer the freedom to commerce and trade and take away a portion of the contract price even without raising any demand or giving any hearing.	None
The Excise Officer cannot under any circumstance give the balance to the ultimate consumer or credit the amount to the Fund.	None
Section 11D is arbitrary and is a colourable piece of legislation and is hereby struck down.	None
Sections 12C and 12D are parts of a device to withhold refunds of unlawfully gathered tax.	None
These provisions are also violative of Article 265 of the Constitution.	None
I express my respectful agreement with the views expressed by my learned Brother Paripoornan, J. that an action by way of a suit or writ petition will be maintainable, depending upon the facts and circumstances of the case.	Decision
I am entirely in agreement with the views expressed by him and the reasoning on Points E, F and G of the concluding part of his judgment.	None
I also agree with my learned Brother Paripoornan, J.s holding on Points H	None
and I subject to my views that in view of Article 265 of the Constitution, the Excise Department is not entitled to withhold refund of any unlawfully collected duty of excise under any circumstances.	None
Any provision to that effect will be ultra vires Article 265 of the Constitution.	None
Such illegally collected duties must be returned to the person from whom it has been collected.	None
In my judgment, the appeals should be allowed and the writ petitions should succeed.	None
There will be no order as to costs.	None
Paripoornan, J. Common questions of law arise for consideration in this batch of cases.	None
Initially the matter came up before a twoMember Bench.	None
The said Bench felt that the decision of the Constitution Bench comprising five Judges in STO v. Kanhaiya Lal Mukundlal Saraf requires reconsideration and referred the matter to a larger Bench of seven Judges.	Issue
When the matter came up before a Bench of seven Judges, it was noticed that Kanhaiya Lal case was expressly approved by a Bench of seven Judges in the decision reported in State Of Kerala v. Aluminium Industries Ltd., Kundara, Quilon.	Issue
and so, by order dated 2871993, the said Bench directed that the matter may be placed before the learned Chief Justice for constituting a still larger Bench.	Issue
That is how this batch of cases came up before a Bench of nine Judges.	Issue
We heard Shri F.S Nariman, Shri Soli Sorabjee and Shri Harish Salve, Senior Advocates, who appeared for the different assessees (claimants) and Shri K. Parasaran and Shri M. Chandrasekharan, Senior Advocates, who appeared for the Union of India.	None
Stated briefly, the controversy centres around the tenability or otherwise of the claim for refund of the amounts paid by way of excise duty under the Central Excises and Salt Act, 1944, now titled as the Central Excises Act, 1944 (hereinafter referred to as the Excise Act) on the ground that it was so done under mistake of law.	Issue
It will be convenient to deal with the controversy by adverting to the minimal facts in the main appeal argued before us  Civil Appeal No. 3255 of 1984  Mafatlal Industries Ltd. v. Union of India.	Facts
The appellant is a textile mill situate at Ahmedabad.	Facts
The appellant and a few other mills manufacture blended yarn.	Facts
The said blended yarn was captively consumed by the various mills for manufacture of fabric, popularly known as art silk fabric.	Facts
For the period prior to 1631972/1731972, the mills paid excise duty on blended yarn manufactured for captive consumption under Tariff Item 18 or 18A of the First Schedule to the Excise Act.	Facts
In Special Application No. 1058 of 1972 filed by M s Calico Mills, who manufactured fabrics and was captively consuming blended yarn produced by it for manufacturing fabric known as art silk fabric, a Division Bench of the Gujarat High Court by judgment dated 1511976, held that the levy of the excise duty on blended yarn prior to 1631972/1731972 under Tariff Item 18 or 18A was clearly ultra vires.	Facts
The High Court directed refund of the excise duty levied for 3 years prior to institution of the petition, which was instituted on 651972.	Facts
The appellant and other millowners stated that as a result of the declaration of the law as aforesaid by the Court, they were not liable to pay excise duty on blended yarn up to 1631972/1731972 and that they had paid the excise duty on the same up to that date under mistake of law.	Facts
They requested for refund of the excise duty so paid till 1631972/1731972, stating that such duty was illegally recovered from them.	Facts
The Revenue did not refund the excise duty as claimed.	Facts
So, the appellant and others filed suits within three years of the aforesaid judgment (1511976) for refund of excise duty illegally recovered from them, with interest.	Facts
The trial court decreed the suits.	Facts
In the appeals filed by the Union of India against the aforesaid decrees passed by the trial court, the High Court of Gujarat allowed the appeals and set aside the decrees passed by the trial court, by judgment dated 641984.	Facts
It was held that in order to successfully sustain the claim of restitution based on Section 72 of the Contract Act, the person claiming restitution should prove loss or injury to him, and in the cases before them, the excise duty paid on blended yarn was ultimately passed on to the buyer of the fabric, and so the claim for restitution will not lie.	Facts
In other words, in cases where an assessee has passed on the duty paid by or released from him, he has suffered no loss or injury, and the action for restitution is unsustainable.	Facts
The aforesaid statement of the law is seriously disputed by the appellants in Civil Appeal No. 3255 of 1984 and others.	Facts
In the ultimate analysis, the main question that falls for consideration in this batch of cases is, whether in an action claiming refund of excise duty (tax) paid under mistake of law, is it essential for the person claiming such refund, to establish loss or injury to him?	Issue
In other words, in cases where the person from whom the excise duty (tax) is collected, has passed on the liability or deemed to have passed on the liability is it open to him to claim refund of the duty paid by him, placing reliance on Section 72 of the Indian Contract Act?	Issue
The further question as to whether an action by way of civil suit or a writ petition under Article 226 of the Constitution will lie, in the light of various amendments to the Act, claiming refund or restitution, also arises for consideration.	Issue
I perused the draft judgment prepared by my learned Brother Jeevan Reddy, J., wherein on the main question, he has held that if the person claiming the refund has passed on the burden of duty to another and has not really suffered any loss or prejudice, there is no question of reimbursing him and he cannot successfully sustain an action for restitution, based on Section 72 of the Indian Contract Act.	None
With great respect, I fully concur with the aforesaid conclusion of my learned Brother.	None
But, in view of the importance of the question raised, I would like to record my own reasons for the aforesaid conclusion.	None
I shall separately deal with the maintainability of the action either by way of suit or petition under Article 226 of the Constitution  the extent to which there is ouster of jurisdiction of courts.	None
In this batch of cases, the claims by different assessees for refund of excise duty paid by them under mistake of law arise over a period of years, and the claims were made in different proceedings  before the departmental authorities, by way of civil suits and writ petitions under Article 226 of the Constitution, which are in appeal before us.	Issue
Broadly, the basis for the various refund claims can be classified into 3 groups or categories The levy is unconstitutional  outside the provisions of the Act or not contemplated by the Act.	None
(II)	None
The levy is based on misconstruction or wrong or erroneous interpretation of the relevant provisions of the Act, Rules or Notifications or by failure to follow the vital or fundamental provisions of the Act or by acting in violation of the fundamental principles of judicial procedure.	None
(III) Mistake of law  the levy or imposition was unconstitutional or illegal or not exigible in law (without jurisdiction) and, so found in a proceeding initiated not by the particular assessee, but in a proceeding initiated by some other assessee either by the High Court or the Supreme Court, and as soon as the assessee came to know of the judgment (within the period of limitation), he initiated action for refund of the tax paid by him, due to mistake of law.	Issue
For the periods during which the refund were claimed, there were different statutory provisions which governed the subject.	None
They are Period up to 781977  	None
Rule 11 of the Central Excise Rules, before amendment Period from 781977 to 16111980  Rule 11 of the Central Excise Rules, as amended Period from 16111980 to 1991991  Section 11A and Section 11B of the then Central Excises and Salt Act Period after 1991991  Section 11A read along with Section 11B of the Act, as amended by Act 40 of 1991.	None
The circumstances and grounds on the basis of which the refund can be claimed, the period within which it should be so done, the forum before which the claim should be preferred and whether the decision thereon is subject to the jurisdiction of ordinary courts, vary from period to period.	None
We shall advert to such provisions and their impact on various aspects regarding the claim for refund a little later.	None
Rule 11 of the Central Excise Rules which dealt with claims for refund of duty as it was in force prior to 781977, is to the following effect No refund of duties or charges erroneously paid, unless claimed within three months.	Reasoning
No duties or charges which have been paid or have been adjusted in an account current maintained with the Collector under Rule 9, and of which repayment wholly or in part is claimed in consequence of the same having been paid through inadvertence, error or misconstruction, shall be refunded unless the claimant makes an application for such refund under his signature and lodges it with the proper officer within three months from the date of such payment or adjustment, as the case may be.	None
It should be noted that Rule 11 before amendment did not provide for any ouster of jurisdiction of courts.	None
We shall deal with Rule 11A as amended and Sections 11A and 11B of the Excise Act a little later.	None
The Revenue states that in view of these later provisions, there is ouster of jurisdiction of courts, relating to claims for refund.	None
The claims by different assessees for refund arose and are were preferred during different periods.	None
After Rule 11 was amended and Sections 11A and 11B were inserted in the Act, the statute contained provisions making them exclusive for claiming refund.	None
Be that as it may, it is only relevant to state at this juncture that in all cases, irrespective of the relevant statutory provisions in the excise act and or the Rules, the claims for refund were made in different proceedings mainly based on Section 72 of the Indian Contract Act.	None
So the main issue, in all the cases, that arises for consideration is, whatever be the nature of the attack regarding the levy, or the basis put forward for claiming refund, or the period for which refund is claimed or the character of the proceedings in which it was so done, or the different nature or character of the statutory provisions either providing or not providing as to how and in what manner the claim should be made,  whether the claim for refund is tenable in any of the proceedings, for any period, based on Section 72 of the Contract Act, if the assessee has passed on the liability to the consumer or third party?	Issue
The levy under the Excise Act is an indirect tax (duty).	None
A duty of excise is levied on the manufacture or production of goods.	None
Ordinarily, it is levied on the manufacturer or producer of goods.	None
(Since the levy is in relation to or in connection with the manufacture or production of goods, it may be levied even at a point later than manufacture or production of the goods.)	None
The duty levied will form part of the total cost of the manufacturer or producer.	None
The levy being a component of the price for which the goods are sold, is ordinarily passed on to the customer.	None
It is a matter of common knowledge that every prudent businessman will adjust his affairs in his best interests and pass on the duty levied or leviable on the commodity to the consumer.	None
That is the presumption in law.	None
The claim for refund in these cases is based upon the plea that excise duty was paid when it was not exigible.	Arguments of Petitioner
It was so done under mistake of law.	Arguments of Petitioner
Refund is claimed basing the action under Section 72 of the Contract Act, which is to the following effect Liability of person to whom money is paid or thing delivered, by mistake or under coercion.	None
A person to whom money has been paid, or anything delivered, by mistake or under coercion, must repay or return it.	None
Illustrations A and B jointly owe 100 rupees to C. A alone pays the amount to C, and B, not knowing this fact, pays 100 rupees over again to C. C is bound to repay the amount to B. A railway company refuses to deliver up certain goods to the consignee, except upon the payment of an illegal charge for carriage.	None
The consignee pays the sum charged in order to obtain the goods.	None
He is entitled to recover so much of the charge as was illegally excessive.	None
Chapter V of the Indian Contract Act is styled thus Of certain relations resembling those created by Contract.	None
The Chapter contains five sections  Sections 68 to 72.	None
The rights and liabilities dealt with in those sections accrue from relations resembling those created by contract.	None
It is not a real contract, but one implied in law or a quasicontract.	None
Law is fairly settled that Money paid under a mistake or on a consideration which has wholly failed or under duress falls under the general head of money had and received.	None
An action for money had and received is an action founded on simple contract which has been called quasicontract or restitution.	None
See Pollock Mulla  Indian Contract and Specific Relief Acts (10th Edn.), p. 598.	None
The Law of Restitution is founded upon the principle of unjust enrichment.	None
As stated by the learned authors, Lord Goff of Chieveley and Gareth Jones in the book The Law of Restitution (3rd Edn.), 1986, It presupposes three things first, that the defendant has been enriched by the receipt of a benefit secondly, that he has been so enriched at the plaintiffs expense and thirdly, that it would be unjust to allow him to retain the benefit.	None
These three subordinate principles are closely interrelated.	None
(p. 16).	None
See also Cheshire Fifoot Furmstons Law of Contract (12th Edn.) 1991, p. 649.	None
The second aspect aforesaid, namely, that the defendant has been enriched at the plaintiffs expense, has been considered by Peter Birks (Professor of Civil Law, University of Edinburgh) in his book Introduction to the Law of Restitution rather elaborately.	None
The principles discernible from the above discussion have been succinctly stated by Endrew Burrows in his book  The Law of Restitution (1993), at p. 16, thus It is the major theme of Birks work that this phrase ambiguously conceals two different ideas in the law of restitution.	None
The first, and most natural meaning, is that the defendants gain represents a loss to the plaintiff in Birks terminology a subtraction from the plaintiff.	None
The second, and less obvious meaning, is that the defendants gain has been acquired by committing a wrong against the plaintiff.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
The person claiming restitution should have suffered a loss or injury.	None
In my opinion, in cases where the assessee or the person claiming refund has passed on the incidence of tax to a third person, how can it be said that he has suffered a loss or injury?	None
How is it possible to say that he has got ownership or title to the amount claimed, which he has already recouped from a third party?	None
So, the very basic requirement for a claim of restitution under Section 72 of the Contract Act is that the person claiming restitution should plead and prove a loss or injury to him in other words, he has not passed on the liability.	None
If it is not so done, the action for restitution or refund, should fail.	None
In this connection, the decision of a threeMember Bench of this Court in Mulamchand v. State of M.P AIR 1968 SC 1218 affords some guidance.	None
The appellant in that case, purchased a right to pluck, collect and remove the forest produce from the proprietors.	None
The right was acquired before the proprietary rights vested in the State of Madhya Pradesh by Act No. 1 of 1951  called the Abolition Act.	None
Acting under the Act, in April 1951 the Deputy Commissioner auctioned the forest produce of villages covered by the purchases of the appellant.	None
Amongst others, the appellant had deposited a sum of Rs 10,000 towards the right to collect lac from the forest.	None
It turned out that the provisions of Article 299 of the Constitution were not complied with and the contract entered into by the appellant therein with the State of Madhya Pradesh was void.	None
The appellant claimed refund on the basis that there was no valid contract.	None
The trial court as well as the appellate court held that the appellant having worked out the contract by collecting the lac from the jungles in pursuance of the agreement, was not entitled to refund of the amount of deposit.	None
In the appeal filed by the appellant, this Court held that if the money is deposited and the goods are supplied or services rendered in terms of the contract, the provisions of Section 70 of the Contract Act may be applicable and, can be invoked by the aggrieved party to the void contract.	None
This Court further held thus (AIR pp. 122223)	None
The juristic basis of the obligation in such a case is not founded upon any contract or tort but upon a third category of law, namely, quasicontract or restitution.	None
In Fibrosa v. Fairbairn 1943 AC 32, Lord Wright has stated the legal position as follows any civilised system of law is bound to provide remedies for cases of what has been called unjust enrichment or unjust benefit, that is, to prevent a man from retaining the money of, or some benefit derived from, another which it is against conscience that he should keep.	None
Such remedies in English law are generically different from remedies in contract or in tort, and are now recognised to fall within a third category of the common law which has been called quasicontract or restitution.	None
In Nelson v. Larholt 1948 1 KB 339, Lord Denning has observed as follows It is no longer appropriate to draw a distinction between law and equity.	None
Principles have now to be stated in the light of their combined effect.	None
Nor is it necessary to canvass the niceties of the old forms of action.	None
Remedies now depend on the substance of the right, not on whether they can be fitted into a particular framework.	None
The right here is not peculiar to equity or contract or tort, but falls naturally within the important category of cases where the court orders restitution if the justice of the case so requires.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
This Court further stated the law thus (AIR p. 1223)	None
It is well established that a person who seeks restitution has a duty to account to the defendant for what he has received in the transaction from which his right to restitution arises.	None
In other words, an accounting by the plaintiff is a condition of restitution from the defendant (See Restatement of the Law of Restitution, American Law Institute, 1937 Edn., p. 634).	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
The observations extracted above indisputably point out that a person who seeks restitution, has a duty to disclose or account for what he has received in the transaction.	None
An accounting is a condition precedent in an action for restitution.	None
By way of analogy, it can be stated that in cases where restitution is claimed under Section 72 of the Contract Act, on the ground of payment due to mistake of law, the person claiming restitution, should plead and prove that he has not passed on the liability to another.	None
That is the nature of accounting in cases falling under Section 72 of the Contract Act.	None
In my opinion, the High Court was justified in law in holding that since the excise duty paid by the appellant was ultimately passed on to the buyers of the fabric, and that the appellant has suffered no loss or injury, the action for restitution based on Section 72 of the Contract Act, was unsustainable.	None
(This is the legal position even under general law, without reference to Section 11B of the Central Excises and Salt Act as amended by Act 40 of 1991.)	Decision
Mr F.S Nariman, Senior Counsel for the appellants, contended that in an action for restitution under Section 72 of the Contract Act, the question as to whether the incidence of duty or tax has been passed on, is an irrelevant factor.	None
There is no such requirement in the statute.	None
The sheetanchor of the appellants case is founded on the decision of the Constitution Bench in Kanhaiya Lal case which was followed by a Bench of 7 Judges in Aluminium Industries case.	None
It was argued that the decision in Kanhaiya Lal case was followed subsequently in Tilokchand Motichand v. H.B Munshi, D. Cawasji Co. v. State of Mysore, Dhanyalakshmi Rice Mills v. Commr. of Civil Supplies 1976 3	Arguments of Petitioner
SCR 387.	Arguments of Petitioner
The plea was that the law laid down in Kanhaiya Lal case has stood the test of time for nearly four decades and there is no requirement either in Section 72 of the Indian Contract Act or in any of the above decisions, holding that in order to claim refund or restitution based on Section 72 of the Contract Act, the liability (duty) should not have been passed on.	Arguments of Petitioner
Our attention was also invited to the decision of the House of Lords in Woolwich Building Society v. IRC (No. 2), of the Canadian Court in Air Canada case, (in particular dissenting judgment of Wilson, J.), of the decision of the Australian Court in Commr. of State Revenue v. Royal Insurance Australia Ltd., of the European Economic Committee in San Giorgio SpA case and the decision of the United States Supreme Court in United States v. Jefferson Electric Manufacturing Co.	Arguments of Petitioner
It was argued that the preponderance of judicial opinion in other jurisdictions also is in favour of the view, that passingon of the liability, is an irrelevant factor for consideration in an action for restitution, and at any rate, it cannot form the basis of a valid defence in an action for restitution.	Arguments of Petitioner
Mr Parasaran, Senior Counsel for the Union of India, contended that the question of passingon of the liability never arose for consideration in Kanhaiya Lal case nor was it decided.	Arguments of Respondent
The said decision cannot be an authority for the proposition that a person claiming refund of tax on the ground of mistake of law is not obliged to allege and prove that it has not been passed on on the other hand, it is mandatory for a claimant in such cases to allege and prove that he suffered a loss or detriment.	Arguments of Respondent
Then and then alone, the Court can grant the equitable relief of restitution.	Arguments of Respondent
The counsel also contended that the principle in Kanhaiya Lal case has not been uniformly followed by this Court subsequently.	Arguments of Respondent
The counsel also distinguished the various foreign decisions that were brought to our notice and highlighted the fact that those decisions were rendered on their own facts.	Arguments of Respondent
The counsel further contended that in cases of indirect levy of tax (cess or fee) which was passed on, this Court has negatived the claim for refund in a few cases.	Arguments of Respondent
Our attention was invited to the following decisions Shiv Shankar Dal Mills v. State of Haryana (SCR p. 1173), State of M.P v. Vyankatlal (SCR pp.	Arguments of Respondent
566, 568), Amar Nath Om Prakash v. State of Punjab (SCR pp. 96100), Indian Aluminium Company Limited v. Thane Municipal Corporation.	Arguments of Respondent
(SCC pp. 488489) and State of Rajasthan v. Novelty Stores AIR 1995 SC 1132 See this order printed on p. 743.	Arguments of Respondent
The main case relied on, Kanhaiya Lal case requires a little detailed examination.	Issue
The respondent, Kanhaiya Lal was a firm.	None
For the Assessment Years 194849, 194950 and 195051, its forward transactions were brought to tax by the assessing authority  the Sales Tax Officer, as per assessment orders dated 3151949, 30101950 and 2281951.	Reasoning
On 2721952, the Allahabad High Court in Budh Prakash Jai Prakash v. STO held that the provisions of the Uttar Pradesh Sales Tax Act, taxing forward contracts were ultra vires the U.P Legislature.	Reasoning
The said judgment was affirmed by this Court on 351954.	None
The attempts of the assessee to obtain refund of tax basing its claim on Budh Prakash Jai Prakash case before the statutory authorities were futile.	None
Thereafter, the assesseefirm filed a writ petition in the High Court, praying to quash the assessment orders, and for direction for refund of tax illegally collected.	Reasoning
By judgment dated 30111956, a learned Single Judge of the High Court, allowed the writ petition.	None
In the appeal, the Revenue contended that since the tax was paid under mistake of law, it was not recoverable.	None
Even so, relying on Section 72 of the Contract Act, the Division Bench affirmed the decision of the Single Judge.	Reasoning
The Revenue took up the matter in appeal before this Court.	None
The pleas of the appellantRevenue, that the assessee should have followed the procedure prescribed by the U.P Sales Tax Act and, that the writ petition filed for refund of money would not lie, were not allowed to be urged by this Court.	None
Mainly, two questions arose before this Court for consideration  Whether the term mistake occurring in Section 72 of the Contract Act took within its fold mistake of law as well as mistake of fact?	None
Whether the tax paid under mistake of law can be recovered under Section 72 of the Indian Contract Act?	None
This Court held that the word mistake occurring in Section 72 of the Contract Act has been used without any qualification or limitation and, so, it takes within its fold mistake of law as well as mistake of fact.	None
On the second question, this Court held that once it is established that the payment, even though it be a tax, has been made by the party under a mistake of law, the party is entitled to recover the same and a party who received the tax is bound to repay or return it.	None
This Court held that there can be no distinction in a tax liability and any other liability on a plain reading of Section 72 and the plea that tax paid by mistake of law cannot be recovered under Section 72, will not be a proper interpretation of the relevant provisions, but to make a law, adding such words as otherwise than by way of taxes after the word paid.	None
The scope of Section 72 was considered only within a limited sphere.	None
It should be noticed that no question was raised before this Court that in order to claim refund (restitution) of sales tax paid,  (an indirect levy)  under Section 72, the claimant should necessarily prove that he has sustained a loss or injury.	None
In other words, the tax collected by him has not been passed on to a third party.	None
Dealing with the plea that the position in law obtaining in England, America and Australia that money paid under mistake of law could not be recovered, and that similar considerations should weigh in interpreting Section 72, the Court held that the true meaning and intent of Section 72 should be interpreted on its own terms, divorced from all considerations, as to what was the state of previous law or the law in England or elsewhere.	None
This Court made further observations to the following effect (SCR pp. 136367) if it is once established that the payment even though it be of a tax, has been made by the party labouring under a mistake of law the party is entitled to recover the same and the party receiving the same is bound to repay or return it.	None
No distinction can therefore, be made in respect of a tax liability and any other liability on a plain reading of the terms of Section 72 of the Indian Contract Act, even though such a distinction has been made in America vide the passage from Willoughby on the Constitution of the United States, Vol. 1, p. 12, op cit.	None
To hold that tax paid by mistake of law cannot be recovered under Section 72 will be not to interpret the law but to make a law by adding some such words as otherwise than by way of taxes after the word paid.	Reasoning
Voluntary payment of such tax liability was not by itself enough to preclude the respondent from recovering the said amounts, once it was established that the payments were made under a mistake of law.	None
On a true interpretation of Section 72 of the Indian Contract Act the only two circumstances there indicated as entitling the party to recover the money back are that the monies must have been paid by mistake or under coercion.	None
If mistake either of law or of fact is established, he is entitled to recover the monies and the party receiving the same is bound to repay or return them irrespective of any consideration whether the monies had been paid voluntarily, subject however to questions of estoppel, waiver, limitation or the like.	None
If, once that circumstance is established the party is entitled to the relief claimed.	None
No question of estoppel can ever arise where both the parties, as in the present case, are labouring under the mistake of law and one party is not more to blame than the other.	None
The other circumstances would be such as would entitle a court of equity to refuse the relief claimed by the plaintiff because on the facts and circumstances of the case it would be for the court to award the relief to the plaintiff.	None
These are, however, equitable considerations and could scarcely be imported when there is a clear and unambiguous provision of law which entitles the plaintiff to the relief claimed by him.	Reasoning
Merely because the State of U.P had not retained the monies paid by the respondent but had spent them away in the ordinary course of the business of the State would not make any difference to the position and under the plain terms of Section 72 of the Indian Contract Act the respondent would be entitled to recover back the monies paid by it to the State of U.P under mistake of law.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
It is apparent that in Kanhaiya Lal case there was no plea by the Revenue that since the assessee has passed on the tax, the claim for refund is unsustainable.	None
Such a question was not posed before this Court for consideration.	None
One of the main aspects to be proved in a claim for restitution, that the person claiming restitution should have suffered a loss or injury in order to sustain an action, was not urged and was not considered.	None
In such a situation the following observations of Lord Halsbury in Quinn v. Leathem 1901 AC 495 (AC p. 506) quoted with approval by a Constitution Bench of this Court in State of Orissa v. Sudhansu Sekhar Misra 1968 2 SCR 154 (SCR p. 162) and again in Orient Paper and Industries Ltd. v. State of Orissa 1991 Supp 1 SCC 81 should govern the matter (SCC p. 96, para 20) there are two observations of a general character which I wish to make, and one is to repeat what I have very often said before, that every judgment must be read as applicable to the particular facts proved, or assumed to be proved, since the generality of the expressions which may be found there are not intended to be expositions of the whole law, but governed and qualified by the particular facts of the case in which such expressions are to be found.	None
The other is that a case is only an authority for what it actually decides.	None
I entirely deny that it can be quoted for a proposition that may seem to follow logically from it.	None
Such a mode of reasoning assumes that the law is necessarily a logical code, whereas every lawyer must acknowledge that the law is not always logical at all.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
The above observations should be borne in mind in understanding the scope of the decision in Kanhaiya Lal case and the cases following the said case.	None
The said decisions cannot be understood as laying down the law that even in cases the liability has been passed on, the assessee can maintain an action for restitution.	Decision
It also appears that there is some inconsistency in the Kanhaiya Lal case.	None
The basis in an action for restitution under Section 72 of the Contract Act, rests upon the equitable doctrine of unjust enrichment.	None
The Court observed on p. 1364 that the recovery of the money paid under mistake of law or fact can be recovered subject however to questions of estoppel, waiver, limitation or the like.	None
Even so, at p. 1366, the Court has observed equitable considerations could scarcely be imported when there is a clear and unambiguous provision of law which entitles the plaintiff to the relief claimed by him.	Reasoning
The very basis of the claim, though statutorily incorporated in Section 72 of the Contract Act, is equitable in nature and if so, how can it be said that equitable considerations should not be applied in adjudicating the claim for restitution (refund)?	None
If an assessee has passed on the tax to the consumer or a third party and sustained no loss or injury, grant of refund to him will result in a windfall to him.	None
Such a person will be unjustly enriched.	None
This will result in the assessee or the claimant obtaining a benefit, which is neither legally nor equitably due to him.	None
In other words, such a person is enabled to obtain an unjust benefit at the cost of innumerable persons to whom the liability (tax) has been passed on and to whom really the refund or restitution is due.	None
The above factors certainly disentitle such a person from claiming restitution.	None
If the decision in Kanhaiya Lal case and the cases following the said decision, enable such a person to claim refund (restitution), with great respect to the learned Judges, who rendered the above decisions, I express my dissent thereto.	Decision
Shri Nariman and Shri Sorabjee also contended that if the relief of refund is withheld or denied on the ground that the assessee has passed on the tax (liability) to the consumer or third party, it will result in a position where the State is enabled to retain and appropriate the unlawful collection to itself.	Arguments of Petitioner
The plea was that Article 265 of the Constitution of India contains a mandate to the effect that no tax shall be levied or collected except by authority of law.	Arguments of Petitioner
It was argued that this is a basic feature of the Constitution and cannot be ignored.	Arguments of Petitioner
If no tax can be collected except by authority of law, the same logic would prevail for retention of amounts collected without the authority of law.	Arguments of Petitioner
Reference was made in this connection to the decision of the Madras High Court in Rayalseema Constructions v. CTO 1959 10 STC 345 Mad (STC pp. 355356) and affirmed by this Court in Deputy Commercial Tax Officer, Madras v. Rayalaseema Constructions 1966 17 STC 505 SC.	Arguments of Petitioner
The plea urged was that, if the assessee, is denied the refund, the State Government could retain the amount illegally collected, and it would amount to violation of the constitutional mandate enshrined in Article 265 of the Constitution.	Arguments of Petitioner
An equitable principle will not hold good against a constitutional mandate.	Arguments of Petitioner
On the other hand the counsel for the Union of India, Shri K. Parasaran, brought to our notice the following portion of the Preamble and Articles 39(b) and (c) of the Constitution to contend that Article 265 of the Constitution cannot be construed in a vacuo or isolation, but should be construed in the light of the basic principles contained in other parts of the Constitution  viz.  	Arguments of Respondent
the Preamble and the Directive Principles of State Policy	Arguments of Respondent
Preamble WE, THE PEOPLE OF INDIA, having solemnly resolved to constitute India into a Sovereign Socialist Secular Democratic Republic and to secure to all its citizens Justice, social, economic and political Articles 39(b) and (c) 39.	None
(b) that the ownership and control of the material resources of the community are so distributed as best to subserve the common good (c) that the operation of the economic system does not result in the concentration of wealth and means of production to the common detriment (emphasis supplied)	None
Mr Parasaran also urged that it should be borne in mind that excise duty is an indirect levy or tax which could be passed on.	None
Innumerable persons bear the brunt.	None
And it is passed on, ordinarily by prudent businessmen.	None
The decisions in R.C Jall v. Union of India (SCR at p. 451) and Province of Madras v. Boddu Paidanna and Sons were referred to.	None
Reference also was made to Section 64A of Sale of Goods Act, 1930 which was substituted later by act 33 of 1963 to show that the levy could be passed on and so recognised by statute, and in the above background, there is a presumption that excise duty has been passed on.	None
The scope of Article 39(b) of the Constitution, as laid down by this Court in State of Karnataka v. Ranganatha Reddy (SCR p. 689), Sanjeev Coke Mfg. Co. v. Bharat Coking Coal Ltd. (SCR pp.	None
102324 1026),	None
State of T.N v. L. Abu Kavur Bai (AIR p. 343	None
SCR pp.	None
759, 761) was highlighted.	None
Reliance was placed on Amar Nath Om Prakash v. State of Punjab (SCR pp.	None
96, 97, 99, 100) Shiv Shankar Dal Mills v. State of Haryana (SCR p. 1173) and Walaiti Ram Mahabir Prashad v. State of Punjab AIR 1984 PH 120 (AIR p. 124), to stress the point that the persons claiming refund who were only middlemen, should not be unjustly enriched and allowed to make a fortune as it were, at the expense of innumerable unidentifiable innocent consumers and that public interest requires that such persons claiming refund should not be unduly or unjustly benefited and, public interest is better served, if the State is allowed to retain the collection of tax, which could be made spent, for the benefit of the public.	None
On an evaluation of the rival pleas urged in the matter, I am of the view that the plea of the counsel for Union of India should prevail.	None
Following the decision in the Province of Madras case and other cases, a Constitution Bench of this Court in R.C Jall v. Union of India stated the nature and character of excise duty, thus (SCR p. 451)	None
Excise duty is primarily a duty on the production or manufacture of goods produced or manufactured within the country.	None
It in an indirect duty which the manufacturer or producer passes on to the ultimate consumer, that is, its ultimate incidence will always be on the consumer.	None
(emphasis supplied) Section 64A of the Sale of Goods Act after its amendment by act 33 of 1963, in providing that in contract of sale amount of increased or decreased taxes, may be added or deducted by the seller or by the buyer, in case of increase or decrease or remitted, after the making of the contract for the sale or purchase of such goods, without stipulation as to the payment of tax where a tax was not chargeable at the time of making the contract, expressly states that the provisions shall apply to any duty of customs or excise and any tax on the sale or purchase of goods.	None
The scope of Article 39(b) of the Constitution which has as its basis the concept of distributive justice, as explained in three cases referred to in the paragraph 305 Ranganatha Reddy, Sanjeev Coke v. Bharat and L. Abu go to show that the words material resources occurring in Article 39 clause (b) will take in, natural or physical resources and also moveable or immovable property and it would include all private and public sources of meeting material needs, and not merely confined to public possessions.	None
So also, the three cases, Shiv Shankar Dal Mills case, Amar Nath Om Prakash case and Walaiti Ram Mahabir Prashad emphasise the principle that the persons who have passed on the burden of the levy  middlemen  should not be allowed to profiteer by illgotten gains and unjustly enriched.	None
An analysis of the above decisions in detail will point out that if Article 265 of the Constitution is literally interpreted and in isolation, and refund ordered, in cases where excise duty has been passed on, it will result in a mockery, totally ignoring the other salient features of the Constitution and the ground realities.	None
As the Preamble states, the Constitution was enacted by the people, to secure to all the citizens, justice, political, social and economic.	None
It is fairly settled by the decisions of this Court, that the Directive Principles contained in Part IV of the Constitution are fundamental in the governance of this country and all organs of the State including the judiciary are bound to enforce those directives.	None
In interpreting the various provisions of the Constitution, the courts have to be realistic and should be alive to the needs of the times.	None
The courts have a responsibility to ensure proper and meaningful interpretation of the Directive Principles and to adjust or harmonise the objectives enshrined in the Preamble  justice, political, social and economic and the Directive Principles contained in Part IV, with the individual rights.	None
In the process, it is permissible to restrict, abridge, curtail and in extreme cases, abrogate other rights in the Constitution, if found necessary and expedient, in particular situations.	None
In the light of the above, I hold that Article 265 should be read along with the Preamble and Articles 39(b) and (c) of the Constitution, and so construed in cases where the assessee has passed on the liability to the consumer or third party, he is not entitled to the claim of restitution or refund.	Decision
The fact that the levy is invalid need not automatically result in a direction for refund of all collections made in pursuance thereto.	Decision
The observation of a threeMember Bench of this Court in Orissa Cement Ltd. v. State of Orissa 1991 Supp 1 SCC 430 (SCC at p. 498, para 69) is apposite in this context.	None
We are inclined to accept the view urged on behalf of the State that a finding regarding the invalidity of a levy need not automatically result in a direction for a refund of all collections thereof made earlier.	None
The declaration regarding the invalidity of a provision and the determination of the relief that should be granted in consequence thereof are two different things and, in the latter sphere, the court has, and must be held to have, a certain amount of discretion.	None
It is a wellsettled proposition that it is open to the court to grant, mould or restrict the relief in a manner most appropriate to the situation before it in such a way as to advance the interests of justice.	None
It is open to the court to deny the equitable remedy of refund (restitution) in such cases.	None
The attempt of persons who have passed on the liability in claiming refund is only to strike at a bargain  to make a fortune at the expense of innumerable unidentifiable consumers.	None
Such persons have suffered no loss.	None
On the other hand, if the State is allowed to retain the amount, it will be available to the community at large and could be made use of for public purposes.	None
On this basis as well, the denial of refund or restitution is valid.	None
There is nothing abhorrent or against public policy if refund or restitution is withheld in such a situation.	None
It should also be stated that in cases of indirect levy of tax which was passed on, this Court has negatived the claim for refund in a few cases, mentioned in para 300 (supra)  	None
Shiv Shankar Dal Mills v. State of Haryana (SCR p. 1173), State of M.P v. Vyankatlal (SCR pp.	Arguments of Respondent
566, 568), Amar Nath Om Prakash v. State of Punjab (SCR pp. 96100), Indian Aluminium Company Limited v. Thane Municipal Corporation.	Arguments of Respondent
(SCC pp. 488489) and State of Rajasthan v. Novelty Stores.	Arguments of Respondent
It now remains to consider the foreign decisions brought to our notice.	None
The various decisions of foreign courts and their scope have been very exhaustively considered by Jeevan Reddy, J. in his judgment under the heading Decisions of foreign courts on the subject.	None
I am in broad agreement with my learned brother Jeevan Reddy, J., in the analysis of the various decisions aforesaid.	None
It is unnecessary to cover that ground all over again.	None
In this context, it would not be out of place to note that academicians have bestowed great thought and in various articles dealt with the matter in sufficient detail, particularly with reference to the foreign decisions brought to our notice.	None
To mention a few, they are When money is paid in pursuance of a void authority.  	None
A duty to repay by Peter Birks Public Law (1992)	None
p. 580	None
Restitution of taxes, levies and other imposts Defining the extent of the Woolwich Principle  by J. Beatson.	None
Law Quarterly Review, Vol. 109, ( 1993)	None
p. 401	None
Restitution of Overpaid Tax, Discretion and Passingon  by J. Beatson.	None
Law Quarterly Review, Vol. 111, (1995)	None
p. 375	None
Notes Unjust Enrichment  by Steve Hedley.	None
Cambridge Law Journal (1995) pp.	None
578599	None
Unjust Enrichment Claims A Comparative Overview  by Brice Dickson Cambridge Law Journal (1995)	None
pp. 100126	None
The Law of Taxation is not an island  Overpaid Taxes and the Law of Restitution  by Graham Virgo British Tax Review (1993)	None
pp.	None
442467 Payments of Money under Mistake of Law	None
A Comparative View  by Gareth Jones Cambridge Law Journal (1993) Comment (225) Restitution, Misdirected Funds and Change of Position  by Ewan McKendrick, Modern Law Review (1992) Vol. 55, pp. 377385	None
In some of the articles, the defences to a claim for restitution of overpaid taxes, has been dealt with in detail.	None
One of them is the article by Graham Virgo appearing in British Tax Review, (1993) (pp. 442467) titled The Law of Taxation is not an island  Overpaid Taxes and the Law of Restitution.	None
At pp.	None
462 and 463 under the subheading Passingon, the learned author has made the following comment Passingon Since restitution at common law is based upon the principle of reversing an unjust enrichment, it is important to determine whether the defendant was actually enriched at the plaintiffs expense.	None
This raises a difficult problem where the Revenue was initially enriched at the taxpayers expense, by virtue of the receipt of overpaid tax, but the taxpayer did not ultimately suffer a loss because the burden of the payment was passed on to somebody else.	None
This could arise if the taxpayer pays excessive VAT and passes the amount overpaid on to customers.	None
As a matter of principle it could be argued that, in such a case, the taxpayer should not be allowed to recover the amount overpaid from the Revenue, because recovery would mean that the taxpayer was unjustly enriched at the expense of those who ultimately bore the burden of the tax.	None
A possible solution to this is to allow those who effectively paid the tax to recover from the taxpayer, who in turn should recover from the Revenue.	None
However, typically in cases of passingon there are many people who effectively bear the burden of the tax and to encourage actions by them would be impractical and unrealistic.	None
Thus, in such cases the best approach is to allow the Revenue a defence of passingon and enable it to retain the tax and use it for the public benefit.	None
However, it remains uncertain to what extent a defence of passingon exists in English law.	None
Such a defence is recognised by European Community law.	None
In Amministrazione delle Finanze dello Stato v. SpA San Giorgio it was held that Community law does not prevent member States from disallowing repayment of charges which have been unduly levied where to do so would entail unjust enrichment of the recipients, for example where the unduly levied charges have been incorporated in the price of goods and passed on to purchasers.	None
Although this decision is confined to charges levied contrary to the rules of Community law, the very fact that Community law accepts the validity of a defence of passingon and accepts that the rationale of it is to avoid the unjust enrichment of the initial taxpayer, is a good reason for the defence to be adopted generally in English law.	None
It would be odd if there were a divergence of approach between English and Community laws on this matter.	None
However, it must be noted that Community law does not prevent member States from adopting a defence of passingon.	None
The San Giorgio case is not authority for the proposition that member States must adopt such a defence.	None
There has been some disquiet expressed as to the need for such a defence in theory and how it would work in practice.	None
The defence was rejected in Mason v. New South Wales 1959 102 CLR 108, Aust HC.	None
The operation of the defence is fraught with difficulties because it is not easy to show that the charge was passed on in the price of goods.	None
For the price of goods is affected by many factors, conditional upon the state of the market.	None
Advocate General Mancini in the San Giorgio case said that the passingon of charges is not generally relevant because of the innumerable variables which affect price formation in a free market and because of the consequent impossibility of definitively relating any part of the price exclusively to a certain cost.	None
Thus, maybe the price of goods was increased in an attempt to recoup the tax paid to the Revenue from the purchasers of goods, but this in turn may have had an impact on sales volume resulting in an overall loss.	None
The burden of the enrichment cannot really be said to have been passed on when the initial taxpayer suffers a net loss.	None
It is submitted that in principle a defence of passingon should exist, with a burden of proving this being on the Revenue it unlawfully demanded the taxes and so it should show that repayment would unjustly enrich the taxpayer.	None
It is unlikely that such a defence would operate successfully in practice in many cases because of the difficulty of proving that the tax was actually passed on.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
Similarly, in the article by J. Beatson (1993) 109 L.Q.R 401, 427428, the learned author has stated regarding passingon, thus Passingon The Law Commission raised the question of whether a payer who has passed on to others, for instance by price increases, the higher cost he has borne because of the overpayment should be precluded from recovering.	None
This defence is permitted by European Community law so long as it does not have the effect of making the right to recover impossible in practice or excessively difficult to exercise.	None
However, it has been criticised, technically because, inter alia, price increases should mean that less will be sold, and also because of difficulties of proof.	None
These difficulties were noted by Lord Goff, and arguments for a similar limit were not accepted by the High Court of Australia in Mason v. New South Wales.	None
However, the underlying rationale of a passingon defence might be achieved by providing, as in the statutes on recovery of value added tax and car tax, that recovery should not be allowed if the payee can show that the payer would be unjustly enriched if he recovered the payment.	None
This would be consistent with the basic equitable features that have influenced the development of the action for money had and received.	None
It is also possible that such a limit would achieve the same policy ends as the reasonable and just limit in provisions such as Section 33 of the Taxes Management Act, 1970 and, if so, it might provide a useful method of achieving a measure of rationalisation.	None
Mention may also be made about the Law Commissions Report in England, Law Consultation Paper No. 120 Restitution of Payments made under a mistake of law  wherein, after discussing the entire caselaw of England and other jurisdictions, an observation is made thus 3.85	None
In principle there would appear to be no reason why such a defence should not apply to cases where the authority can prove on the balance of probabilities that the payer would be unjustly enriched because the charge has been passed on.	None
The views of consultees on the general issue of a passingon defence are invited.	None
In Kanhaiya Lal case (SCR p. 1367)	None
this Court was not inclined to accept the defence in mitigation that the State has not retained the amount, but has spent them away in the ordinary course of governmental activities.	None
This plea in defence based on the theory of Change of Position has been dealt with by Graham Virgo in his article in British Tax Review (1993) at pp. 458459.	None
See also the views expressed in this behalf by a twoMember Bench of this Court in D. Cawasji Co. v. State of Mysore.	None
I am of the view that the above academic opinion has got much force.	None
However, it is subject to one aspect, stated hereunder.	None
As held by me earlier, ordinarily, the presumption is that the taxpayer has passed on the liability to the consumer (or third party).	Decision
It is open to him to rebut the presumption.	Decision
The matter is exclusively within the knowledge of the taxpayer, whether the price of the goods included the duty element also and or also as to whether he has passed on the liability since he is in possession of all relevant details.	Decision
Revenue will not be in a position to have an indepth analysis in the innumerable cases to ascertain and find out whether the taxpayer has passed on the liability.	Decision
The matter being within the exclusive knowledge of the taxpayer, the burden of proving that the liability has not been passed on should lie on him.	Decision
It is held accordingly.	None
The next important question that falls to be considered is, as to what extent the jurisdiction of the ordinary courts is ousted, regarding claims for refund of tax illegally levied or collected?	Issue
According to the Revenue, the Act is a special enactment creating new rights and liabilities and has also made exhaustive provisions, to ventilate the grievances against all illegal and improper assessments by way of appeals, revisions etc. and also to obtain refunds in appropriate cases by following certain procedures and fulfilling some conditions.	Arguments of Respondent
A hierarchy of tribunals is provided to afford relief to the assessees.	Arguments of Respondent
Elaborate alternate remedies provided by the Act, taken along with the specific bar of the jurisdiction of courts provided in Rule 11 (as amended) and Section 11B of the Act, and in particular specifying the conditions and procedure for entertaining claims for refund, period of limitation within which the claim should be preferred, etc., will oust bar the jurisdiction of ordinary courts in that regard.	Arguments of Respondent
(Attention was also drawn to Sections 11C, 11D and also to Sections 12A to D of the Act, to stress the scheme of the Act.)	Arguments of Respondent
On the other hand, counsel for the assesseesclaimants urged that the provisions in the Act dealing with refund of tax unconstitutionally or illegally or unauthorisedly collected are not exhaustive.	Arguments of Petitioner
Even so, in cases where the levy is unconstitutional or illegal or without jurisdiction, the jurisdiction of the civil courts is not barred to annul the levy and or order refund.	Arguments of Petitioner
316.	None
As stated by me earlier in para 290 of this judgment, the claims for refund can be classified broadly into 3 groups.	None
They are (I) the levy is unconstitutional  outside the provisions of the Act or not contemplated by the Act.	Issue
(II) the levy is based on misconstruction or wrong or erroneous interpretation of the relevant provisions of the Act, Rules or Notifications or by failure to follow the vital or fundamental provisions of the Act or by acting in violation of the fundamental principles of judicial procedure.	None
(III) mistake of law  the levy or imposition was unconstitutional or illegal or not exigible in law (without jurisdiction) and, so found in a proceeding initiated not by the particular assessee, but in a proceeding initiated by some other assessee either by the High Court or the Supreme Court, and as soon as the assessee came to know of the judgment, (within the period of limitation) he initiated action for refund of the tax paid by him, due to mistake of law.	Issue
The relevant provisions of law that existed during different periods dealing with the claim for refund are different in content and scope.	None
They are as follows Period	None
up to 781977  Rule 11 of the Central Excise Rules, before amendment Period from 781977 to 16111980  Rule 11 of the Central Excise Rules, as amended Period from 16111980 to 1991991  Section 11A and Section 11B of the Central Excises Salt Act and Period after 1991991  Section 11A read along with Section 11B of the Act, as amended by Act 40 of 1991.	None
Rule 11 of the Central Excise Rules which was in force prior to 781977, has been quoted in para 291 of this judgment.	None
It contains no specific provision relating to ouster of jurisdiction of the courts.	None
Rule 11 of the Central Excise Rules as amended, Section 11A and Section 11B before Amendment Act 40 of 1991 and Section 11B, as amended by Act 40 of 1991, will be more important to consider the question of ouster of jurisdiction of courts.	Issue
Sections 11C, 11D as also Sections 12A to 12D of the Act, will throw light on the scheme of the Act as amended.	Issue
They are as follows (insofar as they are relevant in the instant cases)	None

Any person claiming refund of any duty paid by him may make an application for refund of such duty to the Assistant Collector of Central Excise before the expiry of six months from the date of payment of duty Provided that the limitation of six months shall not apply where any duty has been paid under protest.	None
Explanation.	None
Where any duty is paid provisionally under these rules on the basis of the value or the rate of duty, the period of six months shall be computed from the date on which the duty is adjusted after final determination of the value or the rate of duty, as the case may be.	None
If on receipt of any such application the Assistant Collector of Central Excise is satisfied that the whole or any part of the duty paid by the applicant should be refunded to him, he may make an order accordingly.	None
Where as a result of any order passed in appeal or revision under the Act, refund of any duty becomes due to any person, the proper officer may refund the amount to such person without his having to make any claim in that behalf.	None
