Any person claiming refund of any duty of excise may make an application for refund of such duty to the Assistant Collector of Central Excise before the expiry of six months from the relevant date in such form and manner as may be prescribed and the application shall be accompanied by such documentary or other evidence including the documents referred to in Section 12A as the applicant may furnish to establish that the amount of duty of excise in relation to which such refund is claimed was collected from or paid by him and the incidence of such duty had not been passed on by him to any other person Provided that where an application for refund has been made before the commencement of the Central Excises and Customs Laws (Amendment) Act, 1991, such application shall be deemed to have been made under this subsection as amended by the said Act and the same shall be dealt with in accordance with the provisions of subsection (2) as substituted by that Act Provided further that the limitation of six months shall not apply where any duty has been paid under protest.	None
If, on receipt of any such application, the Assistant Collector of Central Excise is satisfied that the whole or any part of the duty of excise paid by the applicant is refundable, he may make an order accordingly and the amount so determined shall be credited to the Fund Provided that the amount of duty of excise as determined by the Assistant Collector of Central Excise under the foregoing provisions of this subsection shall, instead of being credited to the Fund, be paid to the applicant, if such amount is relatable to (a) rebate of duty of excise on excisable goods exported out of India or on excisable materials used in the manufacture of goods which are exported out of India (b) unspent advance deposits lying in balance in the applicants account current maintained with the Collector of Central Excise (c) refund of credit of duty paid on excisable goods used as inputs in accordance with the rules made, or any notification issued, under this Act (d) the duty of excise paid by the manufacturer, if he had not passed on the incidence of such duty to any other person (e) the duty of excise borne by the buyer, if he had not passed on the incidence of such duty to any other person (f) the duty of excise borne by any other such class of applicants as the Central Government may, by notification in the Official Gazette, specify Provided further that no notification under clause (f) of the first proviso shall be issued unless in the opinion of the Central Government, the incidence of duty has not been passed on by the persons concerned to any other person.	None
Notwithstanding anything to the contrary contained in any judgment, decree, order or direction of the Appellate Tribunal or any court or in any other provision of this Act or the rules made thereunder or any other law for the time being in force, no refund shall be made except as provided in subsection (2).	None
Explanation.	None
For the purposes of this section, (B) relevant date means (f) in any other case, the date of payment of duty.	None
(emphasis supplied) Section 11C deals with the power of the Central Government to dispense with recovery of excise duty in certain specified cases, which is not necessary for our discussion.	None
Section 11D and Sections 12A to D highlight the new scheme of the Act, relating to refund and they are as follows 11D. Duties of excise collected from the buyer to be deposited with the Central Government.(1)	None
Notwithstanding anything to the contrary contained in any order or direction of the Appellate Tribunal or any court or in any other provision of this Act or the rules made thereunder, every person who has collected any amount from the buyer of any goods in any manner as representing duty of excise, shall forthwith pay the amount so collected to the credit of the Central Government.	None
The amount paid to the credit of the Central Government under subsection (1) shall be adjusted against duty of excise payable by the person on finalisation of assessment and where any surplus is left after such adjustment, the amount of such surplus shall either be credited to the Fund or, as the case may be, refunded to the person who has borne the incidence of such amount, in accordance with the provisions of Section 11B and the relevant date for making an application under that section in such cases shall be the date of the public notice to be issued by the Assistant Collector of Central Excise.	None
12A. Price of goods to indicate the amount of duty paid thereon.	None
Notwithstanding anything contained in this Act or any other law for the time being in force, every person who is liable to pay duty of excise on any goods shall, at the time of clearance of the goods, prominently indicate in all the documents relating to assessment, sales invoice, and other like documents, the amount of such duty which will form part of the price at which such goods are to be sold.	None
12B. Presumption that incidence of duty has been passed on to the buyer.	None
Every person who has paid the duty of excise on any goods under this Act shall, unless the contrary is proved by him, be deemed to have passed on the full incidence of such duty to the buyer of such goods.	None
12C. Consumer Welfare Fund.(1)	None
There shall be established by the Central Government a fund, to be called the Consumer Welfare Fund.	None
There shall be credited to the Fund, in such manner as may be prescribed, (a) the amount of duty of excise referred to in subsection (2) of Section 11B or subsection (2) of Section 11C or subsection (2) of Section 11D (b) the amount of duty of customs referred to in subsection (2) of Section 27 or subsection (2) of Section 28A, or subsection (2) of Section 28B of the Customs Act, 1962 (52 of 1962) (c) any income from investment of the amount credited to the Fund and any other monies received by the Central Government for the purposes of this Fund.	None
12D. Utilisation of the Fund.(1)	None
Any money credited to the Fund shall be utilised by the Central Government for the welfare of the consumers in accordance with such rules as that Government may make in this behalf.	None
The Central Government shall maintain or, if it thinks fit, specify the authority which shall maintain, proper and separate account and other relevant records in relation to the Fund in such form as may be prescribed in consultation with the Comptroller and Auditor General of India.	None
It is evident that Rule 11, before amendment, provided a timelimit to apply for refund.	None
Rule 11(4) as amended, Section 11B clauses (4) and (5) before amendment and Section 11B clause (3) after amendment, specifically oust the jurisdiction of the ordinary courts.	None
Detailed provisions are also provided to ventilate the grievances and making such provisions exclusive.	None
Other ancillary or incidental provisions are specified in Sections 11D and 12A to 12D  Section 11D provides that every person, who collects excise duty from the buyer, should deposit the same with the Central Government.	None
It will be adjusted against the duty of excise payable by the person concerned on finalisation of the assessment.	None
Section 11D requires clarification.	None
Excise duty is, ordinarily paid or payable at the time of clearance of the goods.	None
The sale of the goods may be later.	None
So, if excise duty due is already paid by the manufacturer, and later collected by him when the goods are sold, such collection, need not be paid to the Government.	None
Only if the duty has not been paid already or if any excess is collected over and above the duty already paid, then only an occasion arises for payment of the duty collected or excess collected  and this is the purport of	None
Section 11D.	None
The said section (Section 11D) should be understood in the above practical and business sense.	None
Section 12A provides that the price of the goods sold should indicate the amount of duty, which will form part of the price.	None
Section 12B states that the person, who has paid the duty of excise on any goods under the Act, shall be deemed to have passed on the incidence of such duty to the buyer of such goods.	None
It is a rebuttable presumption.	None
Section 12C creates the Consumer Welfare Fund.	None
The amount of duty referred to in Sections 11B(2), 11C(2) and 11D(2) shall be credited in the said Fund.	None
Section 12D provides that the Fund shall be utilised for the welfare of the consumers.	None
The question that falls to be considered is as to how far or to what extent the jurisdiction of the ordinary courts is barred, in view of the alternate remedies provided by the Act by way of appeals, revisions, claims for refund and the period of limitation provided therefor, etc., and specifically excluding the jurisdiction of the civil courts for claiming refund?	Issue
In discussing this aspect, one has to bear in mind the content of Article 265 also.	None
It will apply where the statute is unconstitutional or invalid and also where the collection is unauthorised illegal, i.e, without authority of law.	None
It is settled law that exclusion of the jurisdiction of the civil courts is not to be readily inferred, but that such exclusion must either be explicitly expressed or clearly implied.	Reasoning
There are a few decisions of the Judicial Committee of the Privy Council and innumerable decisions of this Court which have dealt with the matter in detail.	None
I propose to deal, only with the landmark decisions on the subject.	None
In Secy. of State v. Mask Co. AIR 1940 PC 105 the Judicial Committee laid down the law thus (AIR p. 110)	None
It is settled law that the exclusion of the jurisdiction of the civil courts is not to be readily inferred, but that such exclusion must either be explicitly expressed or clearly implied.	Reasoning
It is also well settled that even if jurisdiction is so excluded, the civil courts have jurisdiction to examine into cases where the provisions of the Act have not been complied with, or the statutory tribunal has not acted in conformity with the fundamental principles of judicial procedure.	Reasoning
(emphasis supplied)	None
The scope of the above observation has been explained by a Constitution Bench of this Court, in Firm of Illuri Subbayya Chetty and Sons v. State of A.P The minimal facts in this case will be relevant to understand the scope of the decision.	None
The case arose under the Madras General Sales Tax Act, 1939.	None
Section 18A of the Act provided that no suit or other proceeding shall except expressly provided in the Act, be instituted in any court to set aside or modify any assessment made under the Act.	None
The Act also contained provisions by way of appeals, revisions and further revision to the High Court.	None
The levy under the Act was only on purchase of groundnuts, but the Sales Tax authorities brought to tax the sales turnover and collected tax.	None
The assessee contended that levy of tax on the sales turnover as distinguished from the purchase turnover is illegal, and filed a suit for recovery of the amount so collected.	None
It should be noticed that the assessee himself voluntarily made a return and paid the tax.	None
In such circumstances, the question arose, whether the suit so filed is maintainable in view of the adequate alternate remedies provided by the Act and the ouster of jurisdiction of the courts expressly contained in Section 18A of the Act?	None
On the facts of the case, it was held that the suit was barred.	None
In considering the question of exclusion of jurisdiction of the civil courts to entertain civil actions by virtue of specific provisions contained in the special statute, reference was made to the decision of the Judicial Committee in Secy. of State v. Mask Co.	None
After referring to the observations of the Judicial Committee quoted hereinabove, this Court in Firm of Illuri Subbayya Chetty and Sons v. State of A.P explained the said observations thus (AIR pp. 32526)	None
It is necessary to add that these observations, though made in somewhat wide terms, do not justify the assumption that if a decision has been made by a taxing authority under the provisions of the relevant taxing statute, its validity can be challenged by a suit on the ground that it is incorrect on the merits and as such, it can be claimed that the provisions of the said statute have not been complied with.	None
Noncompliance with the provisions of the statute to which reference is made by the Privy Council must, we think, be noncompliance with such fundamental provisions of the statute as would make the entire proceedings before the appropriate authority illegal and without jurisdiction.	None
Similarly, if an appropriate authority has acted in violation of the fundamental principles or judicial procedure, that may also tend to make the proceedings illegal and void and this infirmity may affect the validity of the order passed by the authority in question.	None
It is cases of this character where the defect or the infirmity in the order goes to the root of the order and makes it in law invalid and void that these observations may perhaps be invoked in support of the plea that the civil court can exercise its jurisdiction notwithstanding a provision to the contrary contained in the relevant statute.	None
In what cases such a plea would succeed it is unnecessary for us to decide in the present appeal because we have no doubt that the contention of the appellant that on the merits, the decision of the assessing authority was wrong, cannot be the subjectmatter of a suit because Section 18A clearly bars such a claim in the civil courts.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
In this case, the relevant Act contained detailed and specific provisions by way of appeal, revision, etc., to ventilate the grievances of the assessees.	None
In addition thereto, there was specific provision ousting the jurisdiction of the courts.	None
Even so, the court did not hold that the principles laid down in Mask Co. case are inapplicable.	None
The principles in Mask Co. case were affirmed and explained.	None
The decision of the Privy Council in Mask Co. case and other decisions of the Privy Council and of this Court, were surveyed in detail by a Constitution Bench of this Court in Dhulabhai v. State of M.P In that case, the assessees filed a suit for refund of the tax on the ground that it was illegally collected from them being against the constitutional prohibition contained in Article 301 of the Constitution of India and not saved in Article 304(a) of the Constitution.	None
Section 17 of the relevant Act was pleaded in defence as a bar to the maintainability of the suit.	None
Section 17 provided that no assessment made and no order passed under the Act or the Rules by any of the statutory authorities, shall be called in question in any case.	None
The Court held that notwithstanding the alternate remedies by way of appeal, revision, rectification and reference to the High Court, the tax therein was levied without a complete charging section and this affected the jurisdiction of the tax authorities, and so, the suit was maintainable, and decreed the suit.	None
After referring to the relevant decisions and in particular, Secy. of State v. Mask Co., Firm of Illuri Subbayya Chetty and Sons v. State of A.P this Court held in para 28 of the judgment, thus (AIR p. 88)	None
The Constitution Bench, however went on to examine the rulings of the Judicial Committee in Mask and Co. and Raleigh Investment Co. cases.	None
Dealing with the former case, this Court pointed out that noncompliance with the provisions of the statute meant noncompliance with such fundamental provisions of the statute as would make the entire proceedings before the appropriate authority illegal and without jurisdiction.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
Referring to the facts of Firm of Illuri Subbayya Chetty and Sons v. State of A.P it was further observed The case of Firm of Illuri Subbayya, may be said to be decided on special facts with additional reference to the addition of Section 18A excluding the jurisdiction of civil court and the special remedies provided in Sections 12A to 12D by which the matter could be taken to the highest civil court in the State.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
This Court also considered the facts and the actual decision of the Special Bench of seven Judges in Kamala Mills Ltd. v. State Of Bombay in detail, with reference to Section 20 of the Bombay Sales Tax Act, 1946, and observed thus The Special Bench refrained from either accepting the dictum of Mask Co. case, or rejecting it, to the effect that even if jurisdiction is excluded by a provision making the decision of the authorities final, the civil courts have jurisdiction to examine into cases where the provisions of the particular Act are not complied with.	None
It is evident from the above, that the principle laid down in Mask Co. case though explained, was not questioned, or departed from, either in Illuri Subbayya Chetty case or Kamala Mills case.	None
In a subsequent decision  Ram Swarup v. Shikar Chand AIR 1966 SC 893 a Constitution Bench of this Court again considered the scope of the decisions in Mask Co. case and Kamala Mills case.	None
Ram Swarup case arose under the U.P (Temporary) Control of Rent and Eviction Act.	None
Section 3(4) of the Act provided that the order passed by the designated authority shall be final and Section 16 thereof further provided that the order passed by the State Government or the District Magistrate, shall not be called in question in any court.	None
In other words, the jurisdiction of civil courts was excluded in relation to the matters covered by orders included within the provisions of Sections 3(4) and 16 of the said Act.	None
The Constitution Bench approached the matter thus One of the points which is often treated as relevant in dealing with the question about the exclusion of civil courts jurisdiction, is whether the special statute which, it is urged, excludes such jurisdiction, has used clear and unambiguous words indicating that intention.	None
Another test which is applied is does the said statute provide for an adequate and satisfactory alternative remedy to a party that may be aggrieved by the relevant order under its material provisions?	None
Applying these two tests, it does appear that the words used in Section 3(4) and Section 16 are clear.	None
Section 16 in terms provides that the order made under this Act to which the said section applies shall not be called in question in any court.	None
This is an express provision excluding the civil courts jurisdiction.	None
Section 3(4) does not expressly exclude the jurisdiction of the civil courts, but, in the context, the inference that the civil courts jurisdiction is intended to be excluded, appears to be inescapable.	None
Therefore, we are satisfied that Mr Goyal is right in contending that the jurisdiction of the civil courts is excluded in relation to matters covered by the orders included within the provisions of Section 3(4) and Section 16.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
Even so, this Court proceeded to state to the following effect (AIR p. 896, para 13)	None
This conclusion, however, does not necessarily mean that the plea against the validity of the order passed by the District Magistrate, or the Commissioner, or the State Government, can never be raised in a civil court.	None
In our opinion, the bar created by the relevant provisions of the Act excluding the jurisdiction of the civil courts cannot operate in cases where the plea raised before the civil court goes to the root of the matter and would, if upheld, lead to the conclusion that the impugned order is a nullity.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
This Court referred to the decisions of the Judicial Committee, in Secy. of State for India in Council v. Roy Jatindra Nath Chowdhury AIR 1924 PC 175 and the decision in Mask Co. and also quoted the observations in the latter case which have been quoted hereinbefore (para 27  supra) and concluded thus In Kamala Mills Ltd. v. State Of Bombay, while dealing with a similar point, this Court has considered the effect of the two decisions of the Privy Council, one in the case of Mask and Co. and the other in Raleigh Investment Co. Ltd. v. Governor GeneralinCouncil, IA at pp. 6263 (AIR at pp. 8081).	None
The conclusion reached by this Court in Kamala Mills case, also supports the view which we are taking in the present appeal.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
It is evident that in Ram Swarup case this Court expressed the view that the decision in Kamala Mills case is in accord with Mask Co. case and the bar of jurisdiction of civil courts cannot operate in cases where the plea raised before the civil court goes to the root of the matter and would, if upheld, lead to the conclusion that the impugned order is a nullity  in other words, where the order or proceeding is attacked as one passed without jurisdiction.	None
Again, the principle laid down in Mask Co. case was only reiterated and observations were made that the decision in Kamala Mills case was in accord with the decision in Mask Co. case.	None
It is important to notice that Gajendragadkar, C.J, spoke for the Bench in all the three decisions Illuri Subbayya Chetty, Kamala Mills and Ram Swarup.	None
In considering Mask Co. and Kamala Mills the Constitution Bench in Ram Swarup case held that if the proceeding assailed is totally invalid and a nullity or without jurisdiction, the jurisdiction of the civil courts is not barred.	None
Again, the principle laid down in Mask Co. was only affirmed.	None
On an analysis of the various decisions, this Court laid down the law thus (Dhulabhai case) (AIR pp. 8990, para 32)	None
Neither of the two cases of Firm of Illuri Subbayya, or Kamala Mills, can be said to run counter to the series of cases earlier noticed.	None
The result of this enquiry into the diverse views expressed in this Court may be stated as follows Where the statute gives a finality to the orders of the special tribunals the civil courts jurisdiction must be held to be excluded if there is adequate remedy to do what the civil courts would normally do in a suit.	None
Such provision, however, does not exclude those cases where the provisions of the particular Act have not been complied with or the statutory tribunal has not acted in conformity with the fundamental principles of judicial procedure.	None
Where there is an express bar of the jurisdiction of the court, an examination of the scheme of the particular Act to find the adequacy or the sufficiency of the remedies provided may be relevant but is not decisive to sustain the jurisdiction of the civil court.	Reasoning
Where there is no express exclusion the examination of the remedies and the scheme of the particular Act to find out the intendment becomes necessary and the result of the enquiry may be decisive.	Reasoning
In the latter case it is necessary to see if the statute creates a special right or a liability and provides for the determination of the right or liability and further lays down that all questions about the said right and liability shall be determined by the tribunals so constituted, and whether remedies normally associated with actions in civil courts are prescribed by the said statute or not.	None
Challenge to the provisions of the particular Act as ultra vires cannot be brought before tribunals constituted under that Act.	None
Even the High Court cannot go into that question on a revision or reference from the decision of the tribunals.	None
When a provision is already declared unconstitutional or the constitutionality of any provision is to be challenged, a suit is open.	None
A writ of certiorari may include a direction for refund if the claim is clearly within the time prescribed by the Limitation Act but it is not a compulsory remedy to replace a suit.	None
Where the particular Act contains no machinery for refund of tax collected in excess of constitutional limits or illegally collected a suit lies.	None
Questions of the correctness of the assessment apart from its constitutionality are for the decision of the authorities and a civil suit does not lie if the orders of the authorities are declared to be final or there is an express prohibition in the particular Act.	None
In either case the scheme of the particular Act must be examined because it is a relevant enquiry.	Reasoning
An exclusion of the jurisdiction of the civil court is not readily to be inferred unless the conditions above set down apply.	Reasoning
(emphasis supplied)	None
Dhulabhai case has been consistently followed by this Court later  see Shree Raja Kandregula Srinivasa Jagannatha Rao Pantulu Bahadur Garu v. State of A.P AIR 1971 SC 71 and other cases.	None
Applying the law laid down in the decisions aforesaid, it is not possible to conclude that any and every claim for refund of illegal unauthorised levy of tax, can be made only in accordance with the provisions of the Act (Rule 11, Section 11B etc.	Decision
as the case may be), and an action by way of suit or writ petition under Article 226 will not be maintainable under any circumstances.	None
An action by way of suit or a petition under Article 226 of the Constitution is maintainable to assail the levy or order which is illegal, void or unauthorised or without jurisdiction and or claim refund, in cases covered by propositions (1), (3), (4) and (5) in Dhulabhai case as explained hereinabove, as one passed outside the Act and ultra vires.	Decision
Such action will be governed by the general law and the procedure and period of limitation provided by the specific statute will have no application.	None
(CCE v. Doaba Coop.	None
Sugar Mills Ltd. 1988 Supp SCC 683 Escorts Ltd. v. Union of india 1994 supp 3 scc 86.)	None
rule 11 before and after amendment, or Section 11B, cannot affect Section 72 of the Contract Act or the provisions of the Limitation Act in such situations.	None
My answer to the claims for refund broadly falling under the three groups or categories enumerated in para 290 of this judgment is as follows Category (I) Where the levy is unconstitutional  outside the provisions of the Act or not contemplated by the Act In such cases, the jurisdiction of the civil courts is not barred.	None
The aggrieved party can invoke Section 72 of the Contract Act, file a suit or a petition under Article 226 of the Constitution and pray for appropriate relief inclusive of refund within the period of limitation provided by the appropriate law.	None
Dhulabhai case, para 32, clauses (3) and (4) Category (II) Where the levy is based on misconstruction or wrong or erroneous interpretation of the relevant provisions of the Act, Rules or Notifications or by failure to follow the vital or fundamental provisions of the Act or by acting in violation of the Fundamental Principles of judicial procedure Under this category, every error of fact or law committed by the statutory authority or tribunal, irrespective of its gravity, or nature of infirmity, will not be covered.	None
It is confined to exceptional cases where the provisions of a particular Act have not been complied with or the statutory tribunal has not acted in conformity with fundamental principles of judicial procedure, as stated in Mask Co. case and in Dhulabhai case.	None
The scope of the above dicta, should be understood in the background of in accord with the observations of the earlier Constitution Bench of this Court in Firm of Illuri Subbayya Chetty and Sons v. State of A.P (AIR at p. 326) to the following effect Noncompliance with the provisions of the statute, to which reference is made by the Privy Council must, we think, be noncompliance with such fundamental provisions of the statute as would make the entire proceedings before the appropriate authority illegal and without jurisdiction.	None
Similarly, if an appropriate authority has acted in violation of the fundamental principles or judicial procedure, that may also tend to make the proceedings illegal and void and this infirmity may affect the validity of the order passed by the authority in question.	None
It is cases of this character where the defect or the infirmity in the order goes to the root of the order and makes it in law invalid and void.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
Dhulabhai case, para 32, clause (1) Here also the appropriate action should be laid within the period of limitation provided by the appropriate law and also can invoke Section 72 of the Contract Act, as the case may be.	None
Category (III) Mistake of law  the levy or imposition was unconstitutional or illegal or not exigible in law (i.e without jurisdiction) and, so found in a proceeding initiated not by the particular assessee, but in a proceeding initiated by some other assessee, either by the High Court or the Supreme Court, and as soon as the assessee came to know of the judgment, (within the period of limitation) he initiated action for refund of the tax paid by him, due to mistake of law In this category, assessees who initiated proceedings and impugned the assessments claimed refund, for any reason, either by way of suit or petition under Article 226 of the Constitution, and the action was dismissed on merits, they cannot maintain an action over again.	Issue
He who fights and runs away, cannot have another day.	None
If the levy or imposition was held to be unconstitutional or illegal or not exigible in law, in a similar case filed by some other person, the assessee who had already lost the battle in a proceeding initiated by him or has otherwise abandoned the claim cannot take advantage of the subsequent declaration rendered in another case where the levy is held to be unconstitutional, illegal or not exigible in law.	None
The claim will be unsustainable and barred by res judicata.	None
(Tilokchand Motichand v. H.B Munshi, Commissioner of Sales Tax.)	None
(This will be confined to the period for which action was laid and lost.)	None
Subject to the above, if a levy or imposition of tax is held to be unconstitutional or illegal or not exigible in law i.e without jurisdiction, it is open to the assessee to take advantage of the declaration of the law so made and pray for appropriate relief inclusive of refund on the ground that tax was paid due to mistake of law, provided he initiates action within the period of limitation prescribed under the Limitation Act.	None
Such assessee should prove the necessary ingredients to enable him to claim the benefit under Section 72 of the Contract Act read with Section 17 of the Limitation Act.	None
Dhulabhai case, para 32, clauses (4) and (5) It should be borne in mind, that in all the three categories of cases, the assessee should prove the fundamental factor that he has not passed on the tax to the consumer or third party and that he suffered a loss or injury.	None
This aspect should not be lost sight of, in whatever manner, the proceeding is initiated  suit, Article 226, etc.	None
As observed earlier, Proposition (1) or clause (1), enunciated in Dhulabhai case should be understood in the background of or in accord with the observations of the earlier Constitution Bench in Illuri Subbayya Chetty case  (AIR at pp. 325326) as quoted in para 321 (supra)  (see para 329 of this judgment).	None
Opinions may differ as to when it can be said that in the public law domain, the entire proceeding before the appropriate authority is illegal and without jurisdiction or the defect or infirmity in the order goes to the root of the matter and makes it in law invalid or void (referred to in Illuri Subbayya Chetty case and approved in Dhulabhai case).	None
The matter may have to be considered in the light of the provisions of the particular statute in question and the factsituation obtaining in each case.	None
It is difficult to visualise all situations hypothetically and provide an answer.	None
Be that as it may, the question that frequently arises for consideration, is, in what situation cases the noncompliance or error or mistake, committed by the statutory authority or tribunal, makes the decision rendered ultra vires or a nullity or one without jurisdiction?	None
If the decision is without jurisdiction, notwithstanding the provisions for obtaining reliefs contained in the Act and the ouster clauses, the jurisdiction of the ordinary court is not excluded.	None
So, the matter assumes significance.	None
Since the landmark decision in Anisminic Ltd. v. Foreign Compensation Commission 1969 2 AC 147 the legal world seems to have accepted that any jurisdictional error as understood in the liberal or modern approach, laid down therein, makes a decision ultra vires or a nullity or without jurisdiction and the ouster clauses are construed restrictively, and such provisions whatever their stringent language be, have been held, not to prevent challenge on the ground that the decision is ultra vires and being a complete nullity, it is not a decision within the meaning of the Act.	None
The concept of jurisdiction has acquired new dimensions.	None
The original or pure theory of jurisdiction means the authority to decide and it is determinable at the commencement and not at the conclusion of the enquiry.	None
The said approach has been given a goby in Anisminic case as we shall see from the discussion hereinafter see De Smith, Woolf and Jowell  Judicial Review of Administrative Action (1995 Edn.)	None
p. 238	None
Halsburys Laws of England (4th Edn.)	None
p. 114, para 67, footnote (9).	None
As Sir William Wade observes in his book, Administrative Law (7th Edn.), 1994, at p. 299	None
The tribunal must not only have jurisdiction at the outset, but must retain it unimpaired until it has discharged its task.	None
The decision in Anisminic case has been cited with approval in a number of cases by this Court citation of a few such cases  Union of India v. Tarachand Gupta Bros. 1971 1 SCC 486 (AIR at p. 1565), A.R Antulay v. R.S Nayak 1988 2 SCC 602 (SCC at p. 650), R.B Shreeram Durga Prasad and Fatehchand Nursing Das v. Settlement Commission (IT WT) 1989 1	None
SCC 628 (SCC at p. 634), N. Parthasarathy v. Controller of Capital Issues 1991 3 SCC 153 (SCC at p. 195), Associated Engineering Co. v. Govt.	None
of A.P AIR 1992 SC 232 , Shiv Kumar Chadha v. Municipal Corpn.	None
of Delhi 1993 3 SCC 161 (SCC at p. 173).	None
Delivering the judgment of a twoMember Bench in M.L Sethi v. R.P Kapur AIR 1972 SC 2379 Mathew, J. in paras 10 and 11 of the judgment explained the legal position after Anisminic case to the following effect The word jurisdiction is a verbal cast of many colours.	None
Jurisdiction originally seems to have had the meaning which Lord Baid ascribed to it in Anisminic Ltd. v. Foreign Compensation Commission, namely, the entitlement to enter upon the enquiry in question.	None
If there was an entitlement to enter upon an enquiry into the question, then any subsequent error could only be regarded as an error within the jurisdiction.	None
The best known formulation of this theory is that made by Lord Dennan in R. v. Bolton 1841 1 QB 66.	None
He said that the question of jurisdiction is determinable at the commencement, not at the conclusion of the enquiry.	None
In Anisminic Ltd., Lord Reid said But there are many cases where, although the tribunal had jurisdiction to enter on the enquiry, it has done or failed to do something in the course of the enquiry which is of such a nature that its decision is a nullity.	None
It may have given its decision in bad faith.	None
It may have made a decision which it had no power to make.	None
It may have failed in the course of the enquiry to comply with the requirements of natural justice.	None
It may in perfect good faith have misconstrued the provisions giving it power to act so that it failed to deal with the question remitted to it and decided some question which was not remitted to it.	None
It may have refused to take into account something which it was required to take into account.	None
Or it may have based its decision on some matter which, under the provisions setting it up, it had no right to take into account.	None
I do not intend this list to be exhaustive.	None
In the same case, Lord Pearce said Lack of jurisdiction may arise in various ways.	None
There may be an absence of those formalities or things which are conditions precedent to the tribunal having any jurisdiction to embark on an enquiry.	None
Or the tribunal may at the end make an order that it has no jurisdiction to make.	None
Or in the intervening stage while engaged on a proper enquiry, the tribunal may depart from the rules of natural justice or it may ask itself the wrong questions or it may take into account matters which it was not directed to take into account.	None
Thereby it would step outside its jurisdiction.	None
It would turn its enquiry into something not directed by Parliament and fail to make the enquiry which Parliament did direct.	None
Any of these things would cause its purported decision to be a nullity.	None
The dicta of the majority of the House of Lords, in the above case would show the extent to which lack and excess of jurisdiction have been assimilated or, in other words, the extent to which we have moved away from the traditional concept of jurisdiction.	None
The effect of the dicta in that case is to reduce the difference between jurisdictional error and error of law within jurisdiction almost to vanishing point.	None
The practical effect of the decision is that any error of law can be reckoned as jurisdictional.	None
This comes perilously close to saying that there is jurisdiction if the decision is right in law but none if it is wrong.	None
Almost any misconstruction of a statute can be represented as basing their decision on a matter with which they have no right to deal, imposing an unwarranted condition or addressing themselves to a wrong question.	None
The majority opinion in the case leaves a court or tribunal with virtually no margin of legal error.	None
Whether there is excess of jurisdiction or merely error within jurisdiction can be determined only by construing the empowering statute, which will give little guidance.	None
It is really a question of how much latitude the court is prepared to allow.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
In a subsequent Constitution Bench decision, Hari Prasad Mulshanker Trivedi v. V.B Raju AIR 1973 SC 2602 delivering the judgment of the Bench, Mathew, J., in para 27 at page 2608 of the judgment, stated thus (SCC pp. 42324, para 28)	None
Though the dividing line between lack of jurisdiction or power and erroneous exercise of it has become thin with the decision of the House of Lords in the Anisminic case Anisminic Ltd. v. Foreign Compensation Commission, 1967 3 WLR 382, we do not think that the distinction between the two has been completely wiped out.	None
We are aware of the difficulty in formulating an exhaustive rule to tell when there is lack of power and when there is an erroneous exercise of it.	None
The difficulty has arisen because the word jurisdiction is an expression which is used in a variety of senses and takes its colour from its context, (see per Diplock, J. at p. 394 in the Anisminic case).	None
Whereas the pure theory of jurisdiction would reduce jurisdictional control to a vanishing point, the adoption of a narrower meaning might result in a more useful legal concept even though the formal structure of law may lose something of its logical symmetry.	None
At bottom the problem of defining the concept of jurisdiction for purpose of judicial review has been one of public policy rather than one of logic.	None
S.A Smith, Judicial Review of Administrative Action, 2nd Edn., p. 98. (1968 Edn.)	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
The observation of the learned author, (S.A De Smith) was continued in its 3rd Edn.	None
(1973) at p. 98 and in its 4th Edn.	None
(1980) at p. 112 of the book.	None
The observation aforesaid was based on the then prevailing academic opinion only as is seen from the footnotes.	None
It should be stated that the said observation is omitted from the latest edition of the book De Smith, Woolf and Jowell  Judicial Review of Administrative Action  5th Edn.	None
(1995) as is evident from p. 229 probably due to later developments in the law and the academic opinion that has emerged due to the change in the perspective.	None
After 1980, the decision in Anisminic case came up for further consideration before the House of Lords, Privy Council and other courts.	None
The three leading decisions of the House of Lords wherein Anisminic principle was followed and explained, are the following Racal Communications Ltd., In re110, OReilly v. Mackman 1983 2 AC 237, Re. v. Hull University Visitor 1993 AC 682.	None
It should be noted that Racal, In re case the Anisminic principle was held to be inapplicable in the case of (superior) court where the decision of the court is made final and conclusive by the statute.	None
(The superior court referred to in this decision is the High Court) 1981 AC 374 (383, 384, 386, 391).	None
In the meanwhile, the House of Lords in Council of Civil Service Unions v. Minister for the Civil Service enunciated three broad grounds for judicial review, as legality, procedural propriety and rationality and this decision had its impact on the development of the law in postAnisminic period.	None
In the light of the above four important decisions of the House of Lords, other decisions of the court of appeal, Privy Council etc. and the later academic opinion in the matter the entire caselaw on the subject has been reviewed in leading textbooks.	None
In the latest edition of De Smith on Judicial Review of Administrative Action  edited by Lord Woolf and Jowell, Q.C Professor of Public Law, 5 Edn.  1995, in Chapter 5, titled as Jurisdiction, Vires, Law and Fact (pp. 223294), there is exhaustive analysis about the concept Jurisdiction and its ramifications.	None
The authors have discussed the pure theory of jurisdiction, the innovative decision in Anisminic case, the development of the law in the postAnisminic period, the scope of the finality clauses (exclusion of jurisdiction of courts) in the statutes, and have laid down a few propositions at pp. 250256 which could be advanced on the subject.	None
The authors have concluded the discussion thus at p. 256	None
After Anisminic	None
virtually every error of law is a jurisdictional error, and the only place left for nonjurisdictional error is where the components of the decision made by the inferior body included matters of fact and policy as well as law, or where the error was evidential (concerning for example the burden of proof or admission of evidence).	None
Perhaps the most precise indication of jurisdictional error is that advanced by Lord Diplock in Racal Communications, when he suggested that a tribunal is entitled to make an error when the matter involves, as many do interrelated questions of law, fact and degree.	None
Thus it was for the county court judge in Pearlman ( Pearlman v. Keepers and Governors of Harrow School, 1979 1	None
All ER 365 ) to decide whether the installation of central heating in a dwelling amounted to a structural alteration, extension or addition.	None
This was a typical question of mixed law, fact and degree which only a scholiast would think it appropriate to dissect into two separate questions, one for decision by the superior court, viz., the meaning of these words, a question which must entail considerations of degree, and the other for decision by a county court, viz., the application of words to the particular installation, a question which also entails considerations of degree.	None
It is, however, doubtful whether any test of jurisdictional error will prove satisfactory.	None
The distinction between jurisdictional and nonjurisdictional error is ultimately based upon foundations of sand.	None
Much of the superstructure has already crumbled.	None
What remains is likely quickly to fall away as the courts rightly insist that all administrative action should be, simply, lawful, whether or not jurisdictionally lawful.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
The jurisdictional control exercised by superior courts over subordinate courts, tribunals or other statutory bodies and the scope and content of such power has been pithily stated in Halsburys Laws of England  4th Edn.	None
(Reissue), 1989 Vol. 1(1), p. 113 to the following effect The inferior court or tribunal lacks jurisdiction if it has no power to enter upon an enquiry into a matter at all	None
and it exceeds jurisdiction if it nevertheless enters upon such an enquiry or, having jurisdiction in the first place, it proceeds to arrogate an authority withheld from it by perpetrating a major error of substance, form or procedure, or by making an order or taking action outside its limited area of competence.	None
Not every error committed by an inferior court or tribunal or other body, however, goes to jurisdiction.	None
Jurisdiction to decide a matter imports a limited power to decide that matter incorrectly.	None
A tribunal lacks jurisdiction if (1) it is improperly constituted, or (2) the proceedings have been improperly instituted, or (3) authority to decide has been delegated to it unlawfully, or (4) it is without competence to deal with a matter by reason of the parties, the area in which the issue arose, the nature of the subjectmatter, the value of that subjectmatter, or the nonexistence of any other prerequisite of a valid adjudication.	None
Excess of jurisdiction is not materially distinguishable from lack of jurisdiction and the expressions may be used interchangeably.	None
Where the jurisdiction of a tribunal is dependent on the existence of a particular state of affairs, that state of affairs may be described as preliminary to, or collateral to the merits of, the issue, or as jurisdictional.	None
(p. 114)	None
There is a presumption in construing statutes which confer jurisdiction or discretionary powers on a body, that if that body makes an error of law while purporting to act within that jurisdiction or in exercising those powers, its decision or action will exceed the jurisdiction conferred and will be quashed.	None
The error must be one on which the decision or action depends.	None
An error of law going to jurisdiction may be committed by a body which fails to follow the proper procedure required by law, which takes legally irrelevant considerations into account, or which fails to take relevant considerations into account, or which asks itself and answers the wrong question.	None
(pp. 119120)	None
The presumption that error of law goes to jurisdiction may be rebutted on the construction of a particular statute, so that the relevant body will not exceed its jurisdiction by going wrong in law.	None
Previously, the courts were more likely to find that errors of law were within jurisdiction but with the modern approach errors of law will be held to fall within a bodys jurisdiction only in exceptional cases.	None
The courts will generally assume that their expertise in determining the principles of law applicable in any case has not been excluded by Parliament.	None
(p. 120) Errors of law include misinterpretation of a statute or any other legal document or a rule of common law asking oneself and answering the wrong question, taking irrelevant considerations into account or failing to take relevant considerations into account when purporting to apply the law to the facts admitting inadmissible evidence or rejecting admissible and relevant evidence exercising a discretion on the basis of incorrect legal principles giving reasons which disclose faulty legal reasoning or which are inadequate to fulfil an express duty to give reasons, and misdirecting oneself as to the burden of proof.	None
(pp. 121122) (emphasis supplied) H.W.R Wade and C.F Forsyth in their book  Administrative Law, 7th Edn., (1994)  discuss the subject regarding the jurisdiction of superior courts over subordinate courts and tribunals under the head Jurisdiction over Fact and Law in Chapter 9, pp. 284 to 320.	None
The decisions before Anisminic and those in the postAnisminic period have been discussed in detail.	None
At pp. 319320, the authors give the Summary of Rules thus Jurisdiction over fact and law	None
Summary	None
At the end of a chapter which is topheavy with obsolescent material it may be useful to summarise the position as shortly as possible.	None
The overall picture is of an expanding system struggling to free itself from the trammels of classical doctrines laid down in the past.	None
It is not safe to say that the classical doctrines are wholly obsolete and that the broad and simple principles of review, which clearly now commend themselves to the judiciary, will entirely supplant them.	None
A summary can therefore only state the longestablished rules together with the simpler and broader rules which have now superseded them, much for the benefit of the law.	None
Together they are as follows Errors of fact Old rule The court would quash only if the erroneous fact was jurisdictional.	None
New rule The court will quash if an erroneous and decisive fact was  (a) jurisdictional (b) found on the basis of no evidence or (c) wrong, misunderstood or ignored.	None
Errors of law Old rule The court would quash only if the error was  (a) jurisdictional or (b) on the face of the record.	None
New rule The court will quash for any decisive error, because all errors of law are now jurisdictional.	None
(emphasis supplied)	None
The scope of the exclusionary clauses contained in the statutes has been considered in great detail with reference to the decisions of the superior courts in England and also the decisions of the Supreme Court of India by Justice G.P Singh (former Chief Justice, M.P High Court) in Principles of Statutory Interpretation, 6th Edn., (1996) at p. 475.	None
The law is stated thus A review of the relevant authorities on the point leads to the following conclusions An Exclusionary Clause using the formula an order of the tribunal under this Act shall not be called in question in any court is ineffective to prevent the calling in question of an order of the tribunal if the order is really not an order under the Act but a nullity.	None
Cases of nullity may arise when there is lack of jurisdiction at the stage of commencement of enquiry, e.g, when (a) authority is assumed under an ultra vires statute (b) the tribunal is not properly constituted, or is disqualified to act (c) the subjectmatter or the parties are such over which the tribunal has no authority to enquire and (d) there is want of essential preliminaries prescribed by the law for commencement of the enquiry.	None
Cases of nullity may also arise during the course or at the conclusion of the enquiry.	None
These cases are also cases of want of jurisdiction if the word jurisdiction is understood in a wide sense.	None
Some examples of these cases are (a) when the tribunal has wrongly determined a jurisdictional question of fact or law (b) when it has failed to follow the fundamental principles of judicial procedure, e.g, has passed the order without giving an opportunity of hearing to the party affected (c) when it has violated the fundamental provisions of the Act, e.g, when it fails to take into account matters which it is required to take into account or when it takes into account extraneous and irrelevant matters (d) when it has acted in bad faith and (e) when it grants a relief or makes an order which it has no authority to grant or make as also (f) when by misapplication of the law it has asked itself the wrong question.	None
With great respect to the learned author, I would adopt the above statement of law, as my own.	None
I would conclude this aspect by holding that the jurisdiction of civil courts is not barred in entirety regarding the attack against the levy and or claim for refund in those cases, coming within the three categories mentioned in paras 290 and 327331 of this judgment, the jurisdiction of the ordinary courts will not be ousted, in the circumstances and subject to the conditions stated therein and in para 332 (supra).	Decision
Two decisions of this Court rendered after Section 11B of the Act was amended in 1991, deserve mention.	Issue
They are  Union of India v. Jain Spinners Ltd. and Union of India v. ITC Ltd.	Issue
In Jain Spinners case the application for refund itself was filed before the statutory authority concerned (Assistant Collector, Central Excise).	None
While the said application was pending, Section 11B of the Act came into force.	None
There was an earlier interim order passed by the High Court directing the deposit of the duty levied with a liberty to the Revenue to withdraw it, subject to the condition that the amount will be refunded if the assessee succeeded ultimately.	None
The Assistant Collector applying the amendments effected in 1991, declined to order refund, holding that the assessee had passed on the incidence of duty to others.	None
It was upheld by this Court notwithstanding the interim orders and other proceedings of the High Court.	None
Basically, the application for refund was filed before the statutory authority concerned, who negatived the claim by giving effect to the provisions of the Amendment Act.	None
There was no attack in the above case that the levy or collection was unauthorised or unconstitutional or without jurisdiction or illegal.	None
In Union of India v. ITC Ltd. the Jain Spinners case was followed.	None
The main aspect that arose for consideration in the latter case was, whether the assessee had passed on the incidence of duty to the consumers or other persons.	None
In spite of the repeated orders of this Court, the assessee failed to establish that the burden of excess excise duty was borne by it and was not passed on to any other person.	None
The assessee had filed five applications for refund.	None
Three of them were allowed by the statutory authorities in the appeals.	None
Only two refund applications were rejected which were assailed in the High Court.	None
The High Court allowed the said applications, directing the Revenue to refund the amounts due as per the two refund applications.	None
In appeal, this Court stressed the fact that the assessee was not able to substantiate that the burden of excess excise duty was borne by it and was not passed on to any other person.	None
Incidentally, this Court also referred to the amended provisions of the Act (Section 11B, Section 12B, etc.) and held that the amended provisions would apply when the matter regarding refund was still pending for adjudication in this Court.	None
In this case also the levy or collection was not assailed as unconstitutional or illegal or without jurisdiction and, in consequence refund was called for.	None
The above two cases did not deal with the maintainability of action in the ordinary courts where the levy or collection is assailed on the ground that it is unconstitutional, illegal or without jurisdiction.	None
The changes brought about by the Central Excises and Customs Laws (Amendment) Act, 1991 (w.e.f 2091991) regarding refund and the scope of Section 11B read with Section 12B was the subject of great controversy before us.	None
The Amendment Act, 1991 is also attacked as unconstitutional, illegal, invalid and unreasonable and as a device to deny refund legitimately due.	None
The relevant statutory provisions have been extracted earlier in this judgment.	None
Briefly stated the position is this Clause (3) of Section 11B provides that notwithstanding any judgment, decree or order of the Appellate Tribunal or any court etc.	None
no refund shall be made except as provided in subsection (2).	None
In other words, the procedure to obtain refund is made exclusive as per Section 11B(3) of the Act.	None
The application, therefore, shall be made under Section 11B(1) and dealt with by the authority concerned under Section 11B(2) of the Act.	None
These provisions mandate amongst other things that the person claiming refund should substantiate that the incidence of duty has not been passed on by him to any other person.	None
The application should also be filed within the time prescribed in the said subsection.	None
Section 11B(2) and Section 11B(3) go together.	None
Under Section 11B(2), in certain specified cases, the duty paid will be refunded to the applicant.	None
One such case is the duty of excise paid by the manufacturer if he had not passed on the incidence of such duty to any other person and substantiates the same.	None
In cases not falling within the proviso to Section 11B(2) of the Act the duty collected will be credited to the Consumer Welfare Fund and the said Fund will be utilised as per Section 12D of the Act.	None
As stated, Section 11B(2) and Section 11B(3) go together.	None
The applications for refund made before the commencement of the Amendment Act, 1991, shall be deemed to have been made under Section 11B(1) of the Act as amended and it shall be dealt with in accordance with Section 11B(2) of the Act.	None
The section contemplates disposal of the applications pending on the date of the Amendment Act as also fresh applications filed after the Amendment Act, 1991 as per the amended provisions.	None
Counsel for the assessees urged that the provisions relating to refund and, in particular, Sections 11B(2) and (3) as amended in 1991 cannot apply to Refund made or due as per orders passed by courts, in a suit or in a petition under Article 226 of the Constitution of India, which have become final.	None
Refunds ordered by the statutory authority concerned which have become final.	None
It is obvious that in such cases no application can or will be deemed to be pending on the date of the commencement of the Amendment Act.	None
No application praying for refund is to be filed in such cases either.	None
No further probe regarding the requisites for obtaining refund specified in the Amendment Act, 1991 is called for in such cases.	None
The above aspects are fairly clear.	None
Sections 11B(2) and (3) cannot be made applicable to refunds already ordered by the court or the refund ordered by the statutory authorities which have become final.	None
It follows from a plain reading of Section 11B, clauses (1), (2) and (3) of the Act.	Decision
The provisions contemplate the pendency of the application on the date of the coming into force of the Amendment Act or the filing of an application which is contemplated under law to obtain a refund after the Amendment Act comes into force.	Decision
I am of the opinion that if the said provisions are held applicable, even to matters concluded by the judgments or final orders of courts, it amounts to stating that the decision of the court shall not be binding and will result in reversing or nullifying the decision made in exercise of the judicial power.	Decision
The legislature does not possess such power.	Decision
The courts decision must always bind parties unless the condition on which it is passed are so fundamentally altered that the decision could not have been given in the altered circumstances.	None
It is not so herein.	None
(Shri Prithvi Cotton Mills Ltd. v. Broach Borough Municipality 1970 1	None
SCR 388 and Madan Mohan Pathak v. Union of India 1978 3 SCR 334.)	None
See also Comorin Match Industries (P) Ltd. v. State Of T.N. 1996 5 SC 167 Alternatively, it may be stated that duty paid in cases, which finally ended in orders or decrees or judgments of courts, must be deemed to have been paid under protest and the procedure and limitation etc. stated in Section 11B(2) read with Section 11B(3) will not apply to such cases.	None
It need hardly be stated that Section 11B(1), the proviso thereto, Section 11B(2) and Section 11B(3) read together will apply only to (1) refund applications made before the amendment of the Act and still pending on the date of commencement of Amendment Act, 1991 and (2) applications contemplated under law to obtain refund and filed after the commencement of the Amendment Act, 1991.	None
(Cases dealt with in paras 290 and 327331 of this judgment will not be covered by the above, to the extent stated therein.)	Decision
Excise duty is an indirect levy.	None
It is intended or presumed to be passed on.	None
This is so under the ordinary law.	None
Section 12B of the Act only provides a statutory rebuttable presumption in that regard.	None
If it turns out that the levy is not exigible, it is refundable to the person who had borne the liability.	None
Ordinarily, in the case of indirect taxes, such persons will be innumerable and cannot be easily identified or located.	None
If the duty, which is not exigible, is refunded to the person who had not borne the liability, it will result in an unjust benefit to him.	None
So the Act has provided in Section 11B(2), that in such cases where the duty is refundable, it will be credited to the Consumer Welfare Fund (Section 12C).	None
However, the proviso to Section 11B(2) provides that the duty of excise will be refunded in a few specified cases, subject to certain conditions  one of them is the manufacturer  in cases, where he has not passed on the incidence to any other person clause (d).	Decision
Those provisions will apply only for refunds to be made under the Act.	Decision
In the totality of the factual situation, it cannot be said that the provisions ushered in by Amendment Act, 1991  and the scheme formulated in Sections 11B and 12A to D  are a device or invalid or arbitrary or unreasonable (except to the extent stated in para 342 supra) or in any way constitutionally infirm.	Decision
(Of course, the cases dealt with in paras 290 and 327331 are excluded to the extent stated therein.)	Decision
Brother Jeevan Reddy, J. has dealt with this matter rather elaborately and there is no need to elaborate the matter any further.	None
In the matter of taxation laws, the court permits a great latitude to the discretion of the legislature.	None
The State is allowed to pick and choose districts, objects, persons, methods and even rates for taxation, if it does so reasonably.	None
The courts view the laws relating to economic activities with greater latitude than other matters.	None
(See Collector of Customs v. Nathella Sampathu Chetty Khyerbari Tea Co. Ltd. v. State of Assam AIR 1964 SC 925 R.K Garg v. Union of India Gauri Shanker v. Union of India 1994 6 SCC 349 Union of India v. A. Sanyasi Rao AIR 1996 SC 1219 etc.)	None
Before closing I should specifically deal with two important aspects.	None
In this judgment I have dealt with cases where duty is paid on items which are consumed as such.	None
Due to paucity of details, the case of captive consumption has not been dealt with.	None
It is made clear that whatever is stated in this judgment will not apply in the cases of goods which are captively consumed.	None
Chapter IIA of the Act was inserted by way of amendment in 1991.	None
The establishment, working, administration and utilisation of the Consumer Welfare Fund is in its stage of infancy.	None
The scheme or setup envisaged by Sections 12C and 12D and its working will require an indepth evaluation by the appropriate authorities in order to vouchsafe that the scheme is not rendered a mere ritual or illusory, but is meaningful and effective.	None
For the present, I do not want to deal with that aspect in detail.	None
For the sake of convenience, I shall summarise my conclusions as hereunder  (in case of doubt, the body of the judgment should be looked into) If the excise duty paid by the assessee was ultimately passed on to the buyers or any other person, and that the assessee has suffered no loss or injury, the action for restitution based on Section 72 of the Contract Act, is unsustainable.	Decision
(This is the legal position even under general law, without reference to Section 11B of the Central Excises and Salt Act as amended by Act 40 of 1991.)	Decision
The decision in Kanhaiya Lal case and the cases following the same cannot be understood as laying down the law that even in cases the liability has been passed on, the assessee can maintain an action for restitution.	Decision
If the decision in Kanhaiya Lal case and the cases following the said decision, enables such a person to claim refund (restitution), with great respect to the learned Judges, who rendered the above decisions, I express my dissent thereto.	Decision
In this context, the observations in paras 329331  clause III shall also be borne in mind.	None
Article 265 should be read along with the Preamble and Articles 39(b) and (c) of the Constitution and so construed in cases where the assessee has passed on the liability to the consumer or third party, he is not entitled to restitution or refund.	Decision
The fact that the levy is invalid need not automatically result in a direction for refund of all collections made in pursuance thereto.	Decision
The presumption is that the taxpayer has passed on the liability to the consumer (or third party).	Decision
It is open to him to rebut the presumption.	Decision
The matter is exclusively within the knowledge of the taxpayer, whether the price of the goods included the duty element also and or also as to whether he has passed on the liability since he is in possession of all relevant details.	Decision
Revenue will not be in a position to have an indepth analysis in the innumerable cases to ascertain and find out whether the taxpayer has passed on the liability.	Decision
The matter being within the exclusive knowledge of the taxpayer, the burden of proving that the liability has not been passed on should lie on him.	Decision
It is not possible to conclude that any and every claim for refund of illegal unauthorised levy of tax, can be made only in accordance with the provisions of the Act (Rule 11, Section 11B etc., as the case may be), and an action by way of suit or writ petition under Article 226 will not be maintainable under any circumstances.	Decision
An action by way of suit or a petition under Article 226 of the Constitution is maintainable to assail the levy or order which is illegal, void or unauthorised or without jurisdiction and or claim refund, in cases covered by Propositions (1), (3), (4) and (5) in Dhulabhai case, as one passed outside the Act, and ultra vires.	Decision
Such action will be governed by the general law and the procedure and period of limitation provided by the specific statute will have no application.	None
The attack against the illegal or unauthorised levy as also the relief of refund may fall ordinarily within the three categories specified in paras 327331 of the judgment.	None
An action by way of suit or writ petition under Article 226 of the Constitution of India will lie in the cases and subject to the conditions stated in paras 327331 and 332 of the judgment.	Decision
The jurisdiction of civil courts is not barred in entirety regarding the attack against the levy and or claim for refund in those cases, coming within the three categories mentioned in paras 290 and 327331 of this judgment, the jurisdiction of the ordinary courts will not be ousted, in the circumstances and subject to the conditions stated therein and in para 332 (supra).	Decision
Sections 11B(2) and (3) cannot be made applicable to refunds already ordered by the court or the refund ordered by the statutory authorities, which have become final.	None
It follows from a plain reading of Section 11B, clauses (1), (2) and (3) of the Act.	Decision
The provisions contemplate the pendency of the application on the date of the coming into force of the Amendment Act or the filing of an application which is contemplated under law, to obtain a refund, after the Amendment Act comes into force.	Decision
If the said provisions are held applicable, even to matters concluded by the judgments or final orders of the courts, it amounts to stating that the decision of the court shall not be binding and will result in reversing or nullifying the decision made in exercise of the judicial power.	Decision
The legislature does not possess such power.	Decision
Alternatively, it may be stated that duty paid in cases, which finally ended in orders or decrees or judgments of courts, must be deemed to have been paid under protest and the procedure and limitation etc. stated in Section 11B(2) read with Section 11B(3) will not apply to such cases.	None
It need hardly be stated, that Section 11B(1), the proviso thereto, Section 11B(2) and Section 11B(3) read together will apply, only to (1) refund applications made under the statute and filed before the amendment of the Act and still pending on the date of commencement of Amendment Act, 1991 and (2) applications contemplated under law to obtain refund and filed after the commencement of the Amendment Act, 1991.	None
(Cases dealt with in paras 290 and 327331 of this judgment will not be covered by the above to the extent stated therein.)	Decision
The proviso to Section 11B(2), provides, that the duty of excise will be refunded in a few specified cases, subject to certain conditions  one of them is the manufacturer  in cases, where he has not passed on the incidence to any other person clause (d).	Decision
Those provisions will apply only for refunds to be made under the Act.	Decision
In the totality of the factual situation, it cannot be said, that the provisions ushered in by Amendment Act, 1991  and the scheme formulated in Sections 11B and 12A to 12D  (in the light of the clarifications made in the body of the judgment, and more particularly in paras 318319 and 344345 above) are a device or invalid or arbitrary or unreasonable (except to the extent stated in para 342 supra) or in any way constitutionally infirm.	Decision
(Of course, the cases dealt with in paras 290 and 327331 are excluded to the extent stated therein.)	Decision
The principles laid down in this judgment should be applied to the factsituation obtaining in individual cases and should be disposed of accordingly.	Decision
The matters may be placed before My Lord the Chief Justice for appropriate orders in this behalf.	Decision
Text of Order in State of Rajasthan v. Novelty Stores.	None
The Orders is of the High Court in the impugned appeals are to be set aside on the sole ground that the respondents after paying octroi duty have passed on the burden to the consumers and collected from the customers on the cloth purchased and sold by them.	None
Therefore, the order of refund would be an unjst enrichment for them.	None
This Court has repeatedly held that such a refund should not be ordered.	None
In that view of the matter without expressing any opinion on the merits during the interrgnum Gram Panchayat was entitled to collect the octroi since respondents are not entitled to the refund of the amount which is already collected and passed on the burden to the consumers these appeals are to be allowed.	None
The appeals are accordingly allowed and the writ petitions in the High Court stand dismissed	None
